

{0}------------------------------------------------

# Short Signatures from DDH without Pairings or Random Oracles

Dario Catalano<sup>1</sup> , Valentina Frasca<sup>1</sup> , Emanuele Giunta<sup>2</sup>

<sup>1</sup> Dipartimento di Matematica e Informatica, Università di Catania, Italy. dario.catalano@unict.it valentina.frasca@phd.unict.it <sup>2</sup> ETH Zurich, Zurich, Switzerland. emanuele.giunta@inf.ethz.ch

Abstract. We present two constructions of short signature schemes based on the polynomial hardness decisional Diffie Hellman. Our simplest scheme guarantees selective security (i.e. the adversary has to commit to the forged message ahead of time) while the second one realizes full fledged existential unforgeability. Remarkably, our schemes can be implemented over standard prime order groups (no pairings needed) and can be proven secure without resorting to the random oracle heuristic.

{1}------------------------------------------------

{3}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-3-0"></span>1 Introduction

Digital signatures are among the most fundamental primitives in cryptography. They allow reassuring the receiver of a digital content that it originates from a specific sender. Moreover, such guarantee can be transferred to any third party (non-repudiation). Because of their centrality, it is of fundamental importance to realize signature schemes that are both secure and efficient.

Security for digital signatures was formally introduced in the seminal paper by Goldwasser et al. [\[GMR88\]](#page-31-0) along with a concrete scheme realizing it. Numerous other works (see below) later improved on it both in terms of concrete efficiency and weaker computational assumptions.

As per the second direction, an important line of research focused on identifying the minimal assumptions needed to realize secure digital signatures. The constructions from [\[GMR88\]](#page-31-0) rely on trapdoor claw free permutations. Subsequently, Bellare and Micali [\[BM92\]](#page-30-0) demonstrated that any trapdoor permutation would suffice. A major advance was achieved by Naor and Yung [\[NY89\]](#page-32-2), who proved that secure digital signatures can be constructed from one-way permutations, thereby eliminating the need for the trapdoor assumption, initially thought to be essential. Finally, Rompel [\[Rom90\]](#page-32-3) further reduced the requirement to the sole existence of one-way functions, which is clearly the minimal necessary assumption.

All the schemes mentioned above, however, fall short in terms of signature size. For instance, [\[GMR88\]](#page-31-0) is based on a binary tree of height d, resulting in a signature size Ω(d · λ). Here, the parameter d is selected so that 2 d exceeds the total number of messages the signer may ever produce. In particular, no scheme with short signature length Θ(λ) is known from symmetric primitives.

Currently, short signatures have been realized from several public-key assumptions such as CDH [\[Wat05\]](#page-32-4), RSA [\[HW09\]](#page-31-1) and SIS [\[DM14\]](#page-31-2). However, with no exception, all constructions in the known prime order group setting either rely on pairings (e.g. [\[BB04,](#page-29-0) [CL04,](#page-30-1) [Wat05\]](#page-32-4)) or the random oracle heuristic ([\[Sch90,](#page-32-5) [PS96\]](#page-32-6)), or are proven under ad-hoc assumptions [\[JMV01\]](#page-31-3).

Our Contributions. We provide the first short and stateless signature schemes that are provably secure under the decisional Diffie Hellman assumption and that do not rely on pairings or random oracles. We list our contributions below.

- Our first scheme guarantees selective security (i.e. the adversary has to provide the message to forge ahead of time) and produces signatures that can be as short as 7 scalars (225B at 128 bits of security).
- Our second scheme instead achieves full EUF-CMA security, although with larger public key, and signatures as short as 9 scalars (290B at 128 bits of security).

Exact signature and verification key sizes are reported in Table [1.](#page-4-1) Both our schemes rely on novel techniques to produce (and simulate in the proof of security) signatures. We consider these among the main contributions of this paper.

{4}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-4-1"></span>

| Scheme | $ \sigma $                            | vk                                                    | Security  |
|--------|---------------------------------------|-------------------------------------------------------|-----------|
| First  | $6\lambda + 4\log q + \log(4\lambda)$ | $O(\lambda^2) \mathbb{G} + O(\lambda^5 \log \lambda)$ | Selective |
| Second | $6\lambda + 6\log q + \log(4\lambda)$ | $O(\lambda^3) \mathbb{G} + O(\lambda^6 \log \lambda)$ | Adaptive  |

Table 1: Parameters achieved by our constructions.  $\lambda$  is the security parameter,  $\mathbb{G}$  is a group of prime order q.

On the concrete efficiency of our schemes. We stress both our schemes are mainly of theoretical interest. They are only meant to demonstrate the feasibility of short signature under plain DDH, and feature public key size and running times that are impractical by all means. However, such high costs stem from our reliance on heavy information-theoretical tools and distance functions over groups [BGI16]. This is in sharp contrast with inefficiency arising from the usage of non black box techniques (such as garbled circuits and iO), leaving room for hope. We therefore leave open the problem of standard model realizations of practical short signatures from the Diffie Hellman assumption.

Strong Puncturability. Due to our proof techniques, both schemes are also strongly puncturable [ADM+24, GVW19], meaning that the verification key can be generated so that for prescribed messages there exists no valid signature with high probability. Specifically, our first scheme support puncturing in a single point, whereas our second construction, with message space  $\mathbb{F}_2^{\mu}$ , can be constrained over any linear subspace. Note the notion in [GVW19] is a stronger than that of [BSW16, CPW20, JDS22] (realizable from OWFs), where given a punctured secret key it is infeasible (as opposed to statistically impossible) to find a signature for punctured messages. To the best of our knowledge, strongly puncturable signatures were only known from RSA, LWE, pairings and simulation sound NIZKs, and our constructions are the first to realize this primitive from the polynomial hardness of DDH.

### <span id="page-4-0"></span>1.1 Technical Overview

Blueprint. Both our schemes follow similar strategies. The verification key contains parameters defining a family of functions  $F_m: \mathcal{X} \to \mathcal{Y}$ , one per message m, such that:

- 1.  $F_m$  is efficiently computable.
- 2. For a (set of)  $m^*$ , programmable in the parameters,  $F_{m^*}$  is  $very \ lossy^3$ .
- 3. The parameters hide the message(s)  $m^*$ .
- 4. For any other  $m, F_m$  is preimage-sampleable given a trapdoor.

<span id="page-4-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>3</sup> Precisely, we will need that  $|\operatorname{Im}(F_{m^*})| \ll |\mathcal{Y}|$ .

{5}------------------------------------------------

Such a family yields a signature as follows. Initially a target  $t \in \mathcal{Y}$  is chosen. A signature for a message m is a preimage  $\sigma$  of t and the verifier simply checks  $F_m(\sigma) = t$ . For most messages such a signature is efficiently computable if  $F_m$  is preimage-sampleable. However, if  $F_{m^*}$  is very lossy, with high probability  $t \notin \text{Im}(F_{m^*})$  and so no signature exists. This gives us selective security when  $m^*$ , the message an adversary attempt to forge, is known in advance, as we can puncture it without having the adversary realize we did so. To argue adaptive security instead we would need F to be puncturable in a set of messages specified by a partitioning scheme, e.g. an admissible hash [BB04].

**Straw man example.** As a stepping stone we present a simple first attempt at instantiating our blue-print, aiming for selective security. The subtle but fundamental reasons why this fails represent the core challenges solved by our schemes.

Key Generation. Key generation consists in the following steps. Given a group  $\mathbb{G}^4$  of prime order q where DDH is hard, one chooses  $\mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{a}_2, \mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{F}_q^n$  and  $\mathbf{t} \in \{0,1\}^n$ , where n is a parameter, and sets<sup>5</sup>  $A_i \leftarrow \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_i$ , for i=1,2. Notice  $A_1, A_2$  are rank-1. Next, a random key k for a PRF  $f_k : \mathbb{G} \to \{0,1\}$  is chosen. The verification key is

$$vk = ([A_1], [A_2], [\mathbf{u}], \mathbf{t}, k)$$

while the secret signing key is  $\mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{a}_2$ . The underlying family of functions  $F_m$  for  $m \in \mathbb{F}_q$  maps two *short* vectors  $\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2 \in \{0, \dots, \ell-1\}^n$  with  $\ell = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  and a field element  $z \in \mathbb{F}_q$  into a bit string of length n as follows:

$$F_m(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) = f_k([A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u} + m(\mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{x}_2)])$$

where we assume  $f_k$  to be applied entry-wise. Following the blue-print, a signature for m would be  $\sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z)$  with  $\mathbf{x}_i$  short such that  $F_m(\sigma) = \mathbf{t}$ . This is efficiently verifiable, as  $F_m$  can be computed efficiently.

Signing. Our approach to sign, i.e. how to preimage-sample each  $F_m$ , is inspired by lock-picking<sup>6</sup>. As is, changing one entry in  $\mathbf{x}_1$  affects all output bits due to the term  $A_1\mathbf{x}_1$ . We wish to reach a state where fixing z and  $\mathbf{x}_2$ , changing  $x_{1,i}$  only affects the i-th entry. This is possible through equivocation: since  $A_1 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1$  then the term  $A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + z\mathbf{u} = (\mathbf{a}_1^{\top}\mathbf{x}_1 + z)\mathbf{u}$  is a Chameleon Hash [KR00]. The idea is then to first fix some valid  $z^*, \mathbf{x}_2$ , brute-force entry-wise  $\mathbf{x}_1$  such that  $f_k([A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u} + \mathbf{x}_1]) = \mathbf{t}$  and finally recover the term  $A_1\mathbf{x}_1$  by finding a collision, done setting  $z = z^* - \mathbf{a}_1^{\top}\mathbf{x}_1$ . Note that, for this to work, one only needs to know  $\mathbf{a}_1$ . This is summarized in the following steps:

<span id="page-5-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>4</sup> See section 2.1 for notation.

<span id="page-5-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>5</sup>  $\otimes$  denotes the Kronecker product, i.e. for  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{F}_q^n$ ,  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}_q^m$  then  $\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{v} = (u_i v_j) \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n,m}$ .

<span id="page-5-2"></span>When picking a physical lock, trying all possible combinations of pin elevations would require exponential time. However, in some cases, applying pressure makes it possible to test each pin's correct elevation *individually* and *independently* from other pins, thus only requiring linear time.

{6}------------------------------------------------

```
1. Sample z^* \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q and \mathbf{x}_2 \leftarrow^{\$} \{0, \dots, \ell-1\}^n;
```

- 2. Call  $(H_1, ..., H_n) = [A_2 \mathbf{x}_2 + z^* \mathbf{u} + m \mathbf{x}_2]; // H_i \in \mathbb{G}$
- 3. For i = 1, ..., n:
- 4. Sample  $x_{1,i} \leftarrow^{\$} \{y : f_k(H_i + my \cdot G) = t_i \land 0 \le y \le \ell 1\};$
- 5. If any of the above sets is empty start again with a different  $z^*, \mathbf{x}_2$ ;
- 6. Set  $z = z^* \mathbf{a}_1^{\top} \mathbf{x}_1$  and  $\sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z)$ ;

Puncturing. Finally we illustrate how security could be argued. As per the blueprint, we wish to setup  $A_1, A_2$  so that  $m^*$  cannot be signed statistically. We may do so setting  $A_1 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1 - m^*I$  and  $A_2 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - m^*I$  with I the  $n \times n$  identity matrix. In this way the function  $F_m$  becomes

$$F_m(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) = f_k([A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u} + m(\mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{x}_2)])$$
  
=  $f_k([(\mathbf{a}_1^\top \mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{a}_2^\top \mathbf{x}_2 + z)\mathbf{u} + (m - m^*) \cdot (\mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{x}_2)]).$ 

For  $m \neq m^*$ , signatures can be pre-sampled as before. However when  $m = m^*$  the second term vanishes and  $f_k$  is applied to elements in  $\mathbb{G}^n$  lying in the linear span of  $[\mathbf{u}]$ . As there are only  $|\mathbb{G}| = q$  many of them, setting  $n \gg \log q$  (e.g.  $n = \log q + \lambda$ ) we have that with high probability  $\mathbf{t} \notin \operatorname{Im}(F_{m^*})$ .

Technical Challenges. To conclude we must argue "regular" and "punctured" keys are indistinguishable. Using DDH this could seemingly be done through 7 hybrids: Initially we only sign with  $\mathbf{a}_1$ . In  $\mathsf{H}_1$  we transform  $A_2$  into a random matrix by DDH<sup>7</sup>. In  $\mathsf{H}_2$  we set  $A_2$  as a random matrix minus  $m^*I$ , which gives the same distribution of  $\mathsf{H}_1$ . In  $\mathsf{H}_3$  we use DDH on reverse, and set  $A_2 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1 - m^*I$ . Finally in  $\mathsf{H}_4$  we switch key, so that  $\mathbf{a}_2$  is now used to sign messages, and mirror the previous steps in  $\mathsf{H}_5$ ,  $\mathsf{H}_6$ ,  $\mathsf{H}_7$  for the matrix  $A_1$ .

Sadly this simple strategy fails in the key-switching hybrid ( $\mathsf{H}_3$  -  $\mathsf{H}_4$ ). The issue is that signatures generated with  $\mathsf{a}_1$  or  $\mathsf{a}_2$  may not have close distributions. Specifically, it can be shown that the *i*-th output bit of  $F_m$  now depends only on z and  $x_{1,i}, x_{2,i}$  (as a consequence of our equivocation approach). Therefore one would need to argue that, fixed z, sampling one randomly and brute-forcing the other produces close distributions. However, both  $x_{1,i}, x_{2,i}$  lie on polynomially small sets, leaving only two options: either  $f_k$  is extremely balanced<sup>8</sup> over the sets of group elements spanned by  $x_{1,i}, x_{2,i}$  for most  $z \in \mathbb{F}_q$ , or even the smallest bias causes the statistical distance to be at least inverse polynomial.

Our Selectively Secure Construction. We overcome the issue above replacing  $f_k$  with a function that is perfectly balanced under some conditions, that is the distance function introduced in [BGI16]. Informally, given a set  $S \subseteq \mathbb{G}$ , the

<span id="page-6-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>7</sup> Here we would actually use Matrix-DDH (also referred to as Rank-DDH), stating rank-1 and random matrices are indistinguishable. This is equivalent to DDH.

<span id="page-6-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>8</sup> Specifically, arranging the points  $[zu_i + mx_{1,i} + (m - m^*)x_{2,i}]$  on an  $\ell \times \ell$  matrix, we would need  $f_k$  to be perfectly balanced when restricted to each row and column.

{7}------------------------------------------------

distance function  $\delta: \mathbb{G} \to \mathbb{N}$  on  $H \in \mathbb{G}$  measures the smallest  $d \in \mathbb{N}$  for which  $H + dG \in S$ .  $\delta_2$ , measuring the parity of  $\delta$  (i.e.  $\delta_2 = \delta \mod 2$ ) is then perfectly balanced since  $\delta_2(H)$  and  $\delta_2(H + G)$  are distinct, at least when  $\delta(H) > 0$ . Note this further means it now suffices to take  $\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2$  as vectors of bits, i.e. we can set  $\ell = 2$ . However, these benefits come at the cost of new technical challenges.

Rejection Sampling. The most notable is that  $\delta_2$  is perfectly balanced on H, H + G only when H has positive distance (or else H and H + G may lie in different buckets), and smaller than some bound  $T = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  (or else computing  $\delta(H)$  may take super-polynomial time). This implies that an inverse-polynomial fraction of group elements breaks  $\delta_2$ . We solve the issue through a rejection sampling strategy: when signing, we reject all values of  $z^*$ ,  $\mathbf{x}_2$  leading to group elements  $[A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u}]$  with either too small or too large (entry-wise) distance.

Message Embedding. Another issue is that previously, to support exponentially many messages, we added a term  $m\mathbf{x}_1 + m\mathbf{x}_2$ , which after puncturing becomes of the form  $(m-m^*)\mathbf{x}_1 + (m-m^*)\mathbf{x}_2$ . However such an approach is incompatible with  $\delta_2$ . The issue is that when signing, given  $H_1, \ldots, H_n \in \mathbb{G}^n$  with good distance, we would need to find  $x_{1,i} \in \{0,1\}$  such that  $\delta_2(H_i + (m-m^*)x_{1,i}) = t_i$ . This fails to make  $\delta_2$  balanced if  $(m-m^*)$  is even, or if  $(m-m^*)$  is too large (as  $H_i$  and  $H_i + (m-m^*)G$  fall in different "buckets").

We solve this relying on an exponentially large family of *short* matrices  $M_m$  with entries in  $\{0,1\}$  so that the difference of any two distinct ones is invertible modulo 2 (i.e. a large exceptional set). Our main function  $F_m$  becomes

$$F_m(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) = \delta_2([A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u} + \mathbf{x}_1 + M_m\mathbf{x}_2)]).$$

where we eventually choose not to add the factor  $M_m$  to  $\mathbf{x}_1$  for technical reasons<sup>9</sup>. Since  $M_m$  is short, if  $[A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}]$  has large enough distance it can be shown the above function equals

<span id="page-7-2"></span>
$$F_m(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) = \delta_2([A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}]) \oplus M_m\mathbf{x}_2 \oplus \mathbf{x}_1. \tag{1}$$

The first term can now be drawn equivocating  $z^* = z + \mathbf{a}_1^\top \mathbf{x}_1$  (resp.  $z^* = z + \mathbf{a}_2^\top \mathbf{x}_2$ ), while  $\mathbf{x}_1$  (resp.  $\mathbf{x}_2$ ) mapping to  $\mathbf{t}$  is found solving a linear system over  $\mathbb{F}_2$ .

Key Switching Lemma. Finally, to show  $\delta_2$  resolves the initial issue, we introduce the key switching lemma, which we see as an independent technical contributions. At a high level our lemma addresses the following problem: two functions  $f_1: \mathcal{X}_1 \to \mathcal{Y}$  and  $f_2: \mathcal{X}_2 \to \mathcal{Y}$  are given. One wishes to sample a collision by setting  $x_1$  uniformly in  $\mathcal{X}_1$  and  $x_2$  from  $f_2^{-1}(f_1(x_1))$ , or vice-versa (i.e.  $x_2$  uniformly in  $\mathcal{X}_2$  and  $x_1$  in  $f_1^{-1}(f_2^{-1}(x_1))$ ). Then our lemma ensures the two distributions are close if both functions are  $\varepsilon$ -balanced<sup>10</sup>.

<span id="page-7-0"></span>This is both to reduce the required minimum distance, and to allow signing 0 even though  $M_0 = \Omega$  will not be invertible in our construction.

<span id="page-7-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>10</sup> i.e. they preserve the uniform distribution within distance  $\varepsilon$ .

{8}------------------------------------------------

To see how this relates to the sampling done for  $F_m$ , observe first that a side effect of  $\delta_2$  is that  $F_m$  splits as the xor of two functions (see equation 1). Letting z absorb the term  $A_1\mathbf{x}_1$ , (i.e. by applying a linear transformation mapping  $z \mapsto z - \mathbf{a}_1^{\top} \mathbf{x}_1$ ) separates the variables  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z)$  from  $\mathbf{x}_1$ . To avoid confusion, we will use  $z^*$  to refer to the shifting of z. Finding a preimage for  $\mathbf{t}$  in this shifted domain is then the same as finding a collision for the two functions

$$f_1: \mathbb{F}_2^n \to \mathbb{F}_2^n$$
  $f_1(\mathbf{x}_1) = \mathbf{x}_1 \oplus \mathbf{t};$   
 $f_2: \mathcal{U}^* \to \mathbb{F}_2^n$   $f_2(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = \delta_2 ([A_2 \mathbf{x}_2 + z^* \mathbf{u}]) \oplus M_m \mathbf{x}_2,$ 

where  $\mathcal{U}^*$  is the set of group elements where  $\delta(A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u})$  is large enough. Our key-switching lemma then ensures keys can be switched as long as both functions are balanced (or regular).

Adaptively Secure Construction. To achieve full adaptive security we augment the selective secure scheme with an algebraic hash, as in [Wat05], so to allow partitioning. This further increases the public key size, but has no effect on signature size. The new verification key contains now (encoding of)  $2\mu + 2$  matrices (with  $\mu$  the message bit-length) and  $\mu+1$  vectors, that are used to map the message to sign into matrices  $[A_i^{(m)}] = [A_{i,0} + m_1 A_{i,1} + \ldots + m_{\mu} A_{i,\mu}]$  and vector  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)}$ . Interestingly this allows to instantiate partitioning from conceptually simpler objects (i.e.  $\mathbb{F}_2$ -linear universal hash functions). This is in contrast to more involved simulation strategies such as the artificial abort of [Wat05]. We defer the details to Section 4.1.

# <span id="page-8-0"></span>1.2 Other Related Work

As mentioned in the introduction, efficient stateless signature schemes have already been constructed in the Random Oracle Model (e.g. [BR93, Sch90, BLS01, GJ03], [KW03, AFLT12]). In the standard model, known constructions of short signatures from prime order groups rely on pairings (e.g. [Wat05], [BB04]). Even if not truly short, it is also worth mentioning the signature schemes of [DN94] and [CD96]: in their work, the authors realize signatures from RSA using flat n-ary trees, as opposed to the binary tree of [GMR88]. A variant of [CD96] from (a variant of) the factoring problem was proposed in [CG05].

The widely recognized hardness of realizing (short) signatures in the standard model from pairing-free groups also fueled a line of research investigating inherent limitations. In [DHH<sup>+</sup>21, CFGG22] it is shown that no signature schemes can be proven secure in Maurer's generic group model (GGM) [Mau94]. These results however rule out constructions making even basic non-black-box usage of the group, including relying on group element representation. Notably, our constructions overcome both results precisely exploiting group element representation through the distance function. A similar picture is observed in the context of several related primitives, e.g. NIZK which are impossible in Maurer's GGM [Giu23] but realizable when the representation is exploited [JJ21].

{9}------------------------------------------------

We also mention that *non-short* signatures from pairing free groups exists. Beyond generic constructions from OWFs, [BKKP15] discuss a tree based scheme from a chameleon hash, which exists if the discrete logarithm problem is hard. The same construction can also be derived through Naor's trick [BF01] from the IBE in [DG17].

# <span id="page-9-0"></span>2 Preliminaries

### <span id="page-9-1"></span>2.1 Notation

 $\lambda$  is the security parameter. A non-negative function  $f: \mathbb{N} \to \mathbb{R}$  is negligible if it vanishes faster than the inverse of any polynomial. For a prime power q,  $\mathbb{F}_q$  is the finite field of order q. Given  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}_q^n$ , then  $\mathbf{u}^{\top} \mathbf{v}$  is their inner product.  $\otimes$  denotes the Kronecker product, so that for  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{F}_q^n$ ,  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}_q^m$  then  $\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{v} = (u_i v_j) \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n,m}$ . Note this implies that  $(\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{x} = (\mathbf{v}^{\top} \mathbf{x}) \cdot \mathbf{u}$ . With abuse of notation we identify  $\mathbb{F}_2$  with  $\{0,1\} \subseteq \mathbb{Z}$ . For  $x,y \in \mathbb{F}_2$ , x+y is their sum in  $\mathbb{Z}$ , while  $x \oplus y = (x+y)$  mod 2 is their sum in  $\mathbb{F}_2$ . We use  $\cdot$  to denote both products in  $\mathbb{Z}$  and  $\mathbb{F}_2$  as there is no ambiguity. Given a group  $\mathbb{G}$  of prime order q with generator G, we use the additive notation over  $\mathbb{G}$  and we denote  $[a] = a \cdot G$  for  $a \in \mathbb{F}_q$ . We extend the notation to vectors and matrices entry-wise.

Given a probabilistic Turing machine  $\mathcal{A}$  the notation  $y \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(x;r)$ , or equivalently  $\mathcal{A}(x;r) \to y$ , means that y is its output on input x and random tape r. We write  $y \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(x)$  if  $\mathcal{A}$  is deterministic. Moreover  $y \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{A}(x)$  denotes the random variable  $y \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(x;r)$  for a uniformly sampled tape r. Given S a set,  $x \leftarrow^{\$} S$  means x is sampled uniformly from S. We convene that  $x \leftarrow^{\$} \emptyset$  means  $x = \bot$ .

Provided discrete random variables x, y and event E, we denote  $H_{\infty}(x)$ ,  $H_{\infty}(x \mid E)$  and  $H_{\infty}(x \mid y)$  respectively the min-entropy of x, the conditional min-entropy of x given E and the average min-entropy of x given y. We defer definitions and known bounds to the appendix, Section A.2.

# <span id="page-9-2"></span>2.2 Statistical Distance

Here we recall the definition of statistical distance, and introduce the class of *bal-anced* functions as those that preserve the uniform distribution up to small statistical distance. Further basic results are deferred to the appendix, Section A.1.

**Definition 1.** Let  $X_1, X_2$  be two random variables over a finite set  $\mathcal{X}$ . The statistical distance between  $X_1$  and  $X_2$  is defined as

$$\Delta(X_1, X_2) := \frac{1}{2} \sum_{x_0 \in \mathcal{X}} |\Pr[X_1 = x_0] - \Pr[X_2 = x_0]|.$$

**Definition 2.** A function  $f: \mathcal{X} \to \mathcal{Y}$  is  $\varepsilon$ -balanced if for  $x \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{X}$  and  $y \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{Y}$ , it holds  $\Delta(f(x), y) \leq \varepsilon$ .

<span id="page-9-3"></span>We now state our key switching lemma, proof of which appears in the appendix, Section B.1. Recall that by our convention (see Section 2.1), sampling from the empty set always returns  $\perp$ .

{10}------------------------------------------------

**Lemma 1 (Key Switching).** Let  $f_1: \mathcal{X}_1 \to \mathcal{Y}$  be a  $\varepsilon_1$ -balanced function and let  $f_2: \mathcal{X}_2 \to \mathcal{Y}$  be a  $\varepsilon_2$ -balanced function. Let  $x_1, x_2, \widehat{x}_1, \widehat{x}_2$  be defined as follows:

- $x_1 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{X}_1 \text{ and } x_2 \leftarrow^{\$} f_2^{-1}(f_1(x_1));$
- $\widehat{x}_2 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{X}_2 \text{ and } \widehat{x}_1 \leftarrow^{\$} f_1^{-1}(f_2(\widehat{x}_2)).$

Then their statistical distance is bounded by  $\Delta((x_1, x_2), (\widehat{x}_1, \widehat{x}_2)) \leq \varepsilon_1 + \varepsilon_2$ .

### <span id="page-10-0"></span>2.3 Universal Hash Functions

Universal Hash Functions (UHF) [CW77] are families of compressing functions. Their security guarantee is that collisions on inputs distributed *independently* from a uniformly sampled UHF key are unlikely (although collisions may be easy to find adaptively). Throughout this paper we rely on families of linear UHF both due to their effectiveness as randomness extractor (see Theorem 1), and their ability to instantiate partitioning schemes (Lemma 2).

**Definition 3.** Let  $\mathcal{X}$  and  $\mathcal{Y}$  be finite sets. A family  $\mathcal{H} \subseteq \{f : \mathcal{X} \to \mathcal{Y}\}$  is a universal hash function (UHF) family if, for any  $x_1, x_2 \in \mathcal{X}$  such that  $x_1 \neq x_2$  and for any  $h \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{H}$ , it holds  $\Pr[h(x_1) = h(x_2)] \leq |\mathcal{Y}|^{-1}$ .

**Proposition 1.** For any prime  $q \in \mathbb{N}$ , and integers n > m in  $\mathbb{N}$  the family  $\operatorname{Hom}(\mathbb{F}_q^n, \mathbb{F}_q^m) = \{f : \mathbb{F}_q^n \to \mathbb{F}_q^m : f \mathbb{F}_q\text{-linear}\}$  is a UHF.

In light of the proposition above we will occasionally abuse notation by identifying  $\mathbb{F}_q^{m,n}$  with  $\text{Hom}(\mathbb{F}_q^n,\mathbb{F}_q^m)$ , and call  $\mathbb{F}_q^{m,n}$  a set of linear UHF.

<span id="page-10-1"></span>Theorem 1 (Leftover Hash Lemma [DORS08]). Let X and Y be random variables over  $\mathcal{X}$  and  $\mathcal{Y}$  respectively,  $m \in \mathbb{N}$  and  $\mathcal{H} \subseteq \{f : \mathcal{X} \to \{0,1\}^m\}$  a UHF family. If  $H_{\infty}(X|Y) \geq m + 2\log(1/\varepsilon)$ , sampling  $h \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{H}$ ,  $U \leftarrow^{\$} \{0,1\}^m$ , it holds

$$\Delta((h, h(X), Y), (h, U, Y)) < \varepsilon.$$

Beyond being useful as randomness extractors, universal hash functions are also an effective tool to instantiate admissible hash functions [BB04]. This is formally implied by the following Lemma. A proof can be found in the appendix, Section B.3.

<span id="page-10-2"></span>**Lemma 2.** Let  $\mathcal{H} \subseteq \{f : \{0,1\}^n \to \{0,1\}^\ell\}$  a UHF family,  $h \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{H}$ ,  $y \leftarrow^{\$} \{0,1\}^\ell$ , and  $m_1,\ldots,m_\vartheta,m^*$  random variables in  $\{0,1\}^n$  and independent from (h,y). Calling Coll :=  $(m_1 = m^* \lor \ldots \lor m_\vartheta = m^*)$ , if  $\ell = \log(2\vartheta)$  and E is an event mutually independent from (h,y) then

$$\Pr\left[h(m^*) = y \land \left(\land_{i=1}^{\vartheta} h(m_i) \neq y\right) \land E\right] \geq \frac{1}{4\vartheta} \cdot \Pr\left[\neg \mathsf{Coll} \land E\right].$$

{11}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-11-0"></span>2.4 Strongly Universal Function

If universal hash functions could be seen as the information-theoretic analog of a collision resistant hash, strongly universal functions (or K-universal) [WC81] are the statistical analog of pseudorandom functions. Informally, the defining property of K-universal functions is being "locally random". That is, given a randomly drawn key, then the output of its associated function on any set of at most K distinct points is truly random. In the following we mildly adapt the original definition to consider ensembles of SUFs, parametrized by  $\lambda$ .

<span id="page-11-2"></span>**Definition 4.** Given  $K : \mathbb{N} \to \mathbb{N}$ , two ensembles of finite sets  $(\mathcal{X}_{\lambda}, \mathcal{Y}_{\lambda})_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  and a pair of PPT algorithms (SUF.Gen, SUF.Eval) such that

- SUF.Gen $(1^{\lambda})$  \$\infty\$ k returns a function key;
- SUF.Eval $(k, x) \to y$  for  $x \in \mathcal{X}$  and  $y \in \mathcal{Y}$ .

then (SUF.Gen, SUF.Eval) is K-universal if for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , given  $k \leftarrow$ \$ SUF.Gen $(1^{\lambda})$  and  $x_1, \ldots, x_{K(\lambda)} \in \mathcal{X}_{\lambda}$  distinct points, setting  $y_i \leftarrow \text{SUF.Eval}(k, x_i)$  then  $(y_1, \ldots, y_{K(\lambda)})$  is uniform in  $\mathcal{Y}_{\lambda}^{K(\lambda)}$ .

We denote  $f_k(\cdot)$  the function SUF.Eval $(k,\cdot)$ . A simple example of K-universal functions is given by the class of polynomials in  $\mathbb{F}_{2^n}$  of degree at most K-1. Up to identifying  $\mathbb{F}_{2^n}$  and  $\{0,1\}^n$  as sets, and truncating the function output, this implies unconditionally the existence of a K-universal function family from  $\{0,1\}^n$  to  $\{0,1\}^m$  for any K,n,m polynomially bounded. For simplicity we will also denote  $\mathcal{K}_{\lambda} = \mathsf{Supp}(\mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}))$  the set of possible SUF keys.

### <span id="page-11-1"></span>2.5 Exceptional Sets of Binary Matrices

A core tool in our construction is a large exceptional set in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n,n}$ . A set  $\mathcal{E} \subseteq \mathbb{F}_2^{n,n}$  is said to be exceptional if for any two distinct  $A, B \in \mathcal{E}$  their difference A - B is invertible. In this section we revisit a construction of an  $\mathbb{F}_2$ -linear immersion  $P^{(\ell)}: \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell} \to \mathbb{F}_2^{n,n}$  for any  $\ell \leq n$  whose image forms an exceptional set.

Up to canonically identifying  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n,n}$  with  $\operatorname{End}(\mathbb{F}_2^n)$  the ring of linear map from  $\mathbb{F}_2^n$  to  $\mathbb{F}_2^n$ , we will actually describe  $P^{(\ell)}: \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell} \to \operatorname{End}(\mathbb{F}_{2^n})$ . To this aim, for every  $\ell \leq n$ , let us introduce the following functions:

- $\psi^{(\ell)}: \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell} \to \mathbb{F}_2^n$  the immersion s.t.  $\psi^{(\ell)}(x_1, \dots, x_{\ell}) = (x_1, \dots, x_{\ell}, 0, \dots, 0);$
- $\phi: \mathbb{F}_2^n \to \mathbb{F}_{2^n}$  an isomorphism of  $\mathbb{F}_2$ -vector spaces.

Given the above, we can define for any  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell}$ ,  $P_{\mathbf{x}}^{(\ell)} : \mathbb{F}_2^n \to \mathbb{F}_2^n$  such that

$$P_{\mathbf{x}}^{(\ell)}(\mathbf{y}) = \phi^{-1} \left( \phi(\mathbf{y}) \cdot \phi(\psi^{(\ell)}(\mathbf{x})) \right).$$

<span id="page-11-3"></span>Where the dot  $\cdot$  denotes the product in  $\mathbb{F}_{2^n}$ . We prove the following theorem in the appendix, Section B.4.

{12}------------------------------------------------

**Theorem 2.** For any  $\ell \leq n$  positive integers, the map  $P^{(\ell)} : \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell} \to \operatorname{End}(\mathbb{F}_2^n)$  assigning  $\mathbf{x} \mapsto P_{\mathbf{x}}^{(\ell)}$  is a well defined  $\mathbb{F}_2$ -linear map. Moreover

$$\mathbf{x} \neq \mathbf{0} \Rightarrow P_{\mathbf{x}}^{(\ell)}$$
 is invertible.

Remark 1. When  $\ell$  is clear from context, e.g. it can be inferred by the length of  $\mathbf{x}$ , we will omit it and simply write  $P_{\mathbf{x}} = P_{\mathbf{x}}^{(\ell)}$ .

# <span id="page-12-0"></span>2.6 Prime Order Groups

In this section we recall notation for cryptographic prime order groups, along with the decisional Diffie-Hellman problem and equivalent formulations up to logarithmic loss in the instance size.

**Definition 5.** A cryptographic prime order group is a PPT algorithm G.Gen such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , G.Gen $(1^{\lambda})$   $^{\$} \rightarrow (\mathbb{G}, G, q)$  where  $\mathbb{G}$  is a group with efficiently computable operation and membership test,  $G \in \mathbb{G}$  is a generator and  $q = |\mathbb{G}|$  is prime.

Without loss of generality we will assume G, q to be embedded in  $\mathbb{G}$ . Moreover, we will denote  $\mathcal{G}_{\lambda} = \mathsf{Supp}(\mathsf{G}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}))$  the set of possible group descriptions.

**Definition 6.** The decisional Diffie-Hellman (DDH) problem is hard in G.Gen if for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible  $\varepsilon$  such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , sampling  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \leftarrow^{\$} G.Gen(1^{\lambda})$ ,  $a, b, c \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q$  then  $Adv_{G.\mathcal{A}}^{ddh}(\lambda) :=$ 

$$|\Pr\left[1 \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathbb{G}, [a], [b], [ab])\right] - \Pr\left[1 \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathbb{G}, [a], [b], [c])\right]| \leq \varepsilon(\lambda).$$

<span id="page-12-1"></span>**Definition 7.** The (n,m)-Matrix Diffie-Hellman problem, or (n,m)-MDDH, for  $n \leq m$  polynomially bounded functions, is hard for G.Gen if for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible  $\varepsilon$  such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , sampling matrices  $A \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n(\lambda),m(\lambda)}$  and  $B \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n(\lambda),m(\lambda)}$  with  $\mathsf{rk}(A) = m(\lambda)$  and  $\mathsf{rk}(B) = 1$  then,

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{rank}}_{\mathsf{G},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \; \coloneqq \; |\Pr\left[1 \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathbb{G},[A])\right] - \Pr\left[1 \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathbb{G},[B])\right]| \; \leq \; \varepsilon(\lambda).$$

Note that the DDH problem and the (n,m)-MDDH problem are equivalent for all polynomially bounded n,m. An elegant and optimal reduction of (n,m)-MDDH to DDH losing only a factor  $\log_2 n$  is given in [Vil12].

In order to simplify the analysis in our reductions, we will often consider the above decisional problem with mildly different distributions. Specifically, in Section B.5 of the appendix we quantify the statistical loss when sampling A uniformly in  $\mathbb{F}_q^{n,m}$  and B as  $\mathbf{u} \otimes (1,\mathbf{v})$  or  $\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{w}$  for uniformly sampled vectors of matching dimensions.

{13}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-13-0"></span>2.7 Digital Signatures

The standard security notion for digital signature is existential unforgeability against chosen message attacks [GMR88], recalled in this section. However, in this paper we further consider a *selective* variant where the adversary has to choose the forged message before seeing the public parameters. Note our first (more efficient) construction will be proven selectively secure, whereas our second one achieves EUF-CMA security.

**Definition 8.** A signature scheme is a triplet of PPT algorithms (S.Gen, S.Sign, S.Vfy) along with an ensemble of sets  $(\mathcal{M}_{\lambda})_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  (the message space) such that:

- S.Gen $(1^{\lambda})$  \$\infty\$ (vk, sk) generates a verification key and a signing key;
- S.Sign(sk, m)  $^{\$} \rightarrow \sigma$  returns the signature of a message  $m \in \mathcal{M}_{\lambda}$ ;
- S.Vfy(vk,  $m, \sigma$ )  $\rightarrow 0/1$  verifies the validity of  $\sigma$  for m.

Without loss of generality, up to relying on the hash-and-sign paradigm, we can always assume  $\mathcal{M}_{\lambda} = \{0,1\}^{2\lambda}$ .

**Definition 9.** A signature scheme (S.Gen, S.Sign, S.Vfy) is correct if there exists a negligible function  $\varepsilon$  such that for all messages  $m \in \mathcal{M}_{\lambda}$ , then

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{S.Vfy}(\mathsf{vk}, m, \sigma) \to 0 \; \left| \begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S.Gen}(1^{\lambda}) \\ \sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S.Sign}(\mathsf{sk}, m) \end{array} \right] \; \leq \; \varepsilon(\lambda).$$

**Definition 10.** A signature scheme S is selectively unforgeable under chosen message attack (SUF-CMA) if for any PPT adversary A executed in the experiment in Figure 1 (left) there exists a negligible  $\varepsilon$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{suf\text{-}cma}}_{\mathsf{S},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \; \coloneqq \; \Pr\left[\mathsf{Exp}^{\mathsf{suf\text{-}cma}}_{\mathsf{S},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) = 1\right] \; \le \; \varepsilon(\lambda).$$

Analogously S is existentially unforgeable under chosen message attacks (EUF-CMA) if for any PPT adversary A executed in the experiment in Figure 1 (right) there exists a negligible  $\varepsilon$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{euf\text{-}cma}}_{\mathsf{S},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \; \coloneqq \; \Pr\left[\mathsf{Exp}^{\mathsf{euf\text{-}cma}}_{\mathsf{S},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) = 1\right] \; \le \; \varepsilon(\lambda).$$

# <span id="page-13-1"></span>3 Distance Function

### <span id="page-13-2"></span>3.1 Definition and Properties

In this section we recall the definition of distance function over a group  $\mathbb{G}$ , a tool originally introduced in the context of homomorphic secret sharing [BGI16]. Given a subset  $S \subseteq \mathbb{G}$ , a distance function measures how far a given group H element is from S. This is the smallest  $d \in \mathbb{N}$  such that  $H+dG \in S$ . Concretely, S must be efficiently representable and decidable. Moreover, for the distance to be

{14}------------------------------------------------

Fig. 1: Selective unforgeability (left) and existential unforgeability (right) game under chosen message attacks, for scheme S and adversary A.

computable in PPT-time, S should not have many large gaps. All these properties can be achieved through a SUF<sup>11</sup>: given k a key for  $f_k : \mathbb{G} \to \{0,1\}^L$ , simply let  $S = f_k^{-1}(0)$ . Choosing  $L = O(\log \lambda)$  then ensures that the average element has distance proportional to  $2^L = \operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$ , which can be measured via brute force search. This is formalized in Definition 11, where we assume (SUF.Gen, SUF.Eval) to be a strongly universal function family so that  $f_k$  for  $k \in \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$  is well defined over all  $\mathbb{G} \subseteq \{0,1\}^*$  with  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ .

<span id="page-14-3"></span><span id="page-14-2"></span>**Definition 11.** Given  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ ,  $k \in \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$  and  $L, T = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , we define the distance function  $\delta_{\mathsf{dp}} : \mathbb{G} \to \mathbb{N} \cup \{\bot\}$  as the output of the algorithm in Figure 2, where  $\mathsf{dp} = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k, L, T)$ .

```
\begin{aligned} & \frac{\delta_{\sf dp}(H):}{1: & \operatorname{Parse} \; \sf dp = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k, L, T)} \ & 2: & \mathbf{For} \; i \in \{0, \dots, T-1\}: \ & 3: & \mathbf{If} \; f_k(H+i\cdot G) = 0^L: \mathbf{Return} \; i \ & 4: & \mathbf{Return} \; \bot \end{aligned}
```

Fig. 2: Distance function with  $f_k : \mathbb{G} \to \{0,1\}^L$  a SUF and  $H \in \mathbb{G}$ .

When clear from the context, we will omit dp and write  $\delta : \mathbb{G} \to \mathbb{N} \cup \{\bot\}$ . Given a distance function  $\delta$ , we extend it to  $\mathbb{G}^n$  by applying it entry-wise, i.e.

$$\delta: \mathbb{G}^n \to (\mathbb{N} \cup \{\bot\})^n \qquad \delta(H_1, \ldots, H_n) = (\delta(H_1), \ldots, \delta(H_n)).$$

<span id="page-14-1"></span>Previous work (e.g. [BGI16], [DGI<sup>+</sup>19]) relied on pseudorandom functions instead. We opt for strongly universal functions instead for the sake of a simpler analysis.

{15}------------------------------------------------

Moreover, the following associated functions will be useful:

$$\delta_{\min}: \mathbb{G}^n \to \mathbb{N} \cup \{\bot\} \qquad \delta_{\min}(H_1, \dots, H_n) = \min_{i=1}^n \delta(H_i)$$
$$\delta_2: \mathbb{G}^n \to (\mathbb{F}_2 \cup \{\bot\})^n \qquad \delta_2(H_1, \dots, H_n) = \delta(H_1, \dots, H_n) \mod 2.$$

where we assume that min returns ⊥ if at least one of its arguments is ⊥, and that ⊥ modulo 2 is mapped to ⊥. To avoid explicitly requiring the distance to be an integer, throughout the paper we also assume that ⊥ ≰ d and ⊥ ≱ d for any integer d. In particular δmin(H) ≥ d also means δmin(H) ̸= ⊥.

Finally, the main property of the distance function is its restricted homomorphism, as formally stated in the following propositions. Their proofs appear in the appendix, Section [C.1.](#page-41-1)

Lemma 3. Let dp = (G, G, q, k, L, T) as in Definition [11.](#page-14-2) For any H ∈ G<sup>n</sup> with δmin(H) ≥ d and vector x ∈ N <sup>n</sup> with ∥x∥<sup>∞</sup> ≤ d we have

<span id="page-15-1"></span>
$$\delta(\mathbf{H} + \mathbf{x} \cdot G) = \delta(\mathbf{H}) - \mathbf{x}.$$

In particular, under the same assumptions δ2(H + xG) = δ2(H) ⊕ x.

<span id="page-15-2"></span>Corollary 1. Let dp = (G, G, q, k, L, T) as in Definition [11.](#page-14-2) For any H ∈ G<sup>n</sup> and pair of vectors x1, x<sup>2</sup> ∈ N <sup>n</sup> such that δmin(H − x1G) ≥ max(∥x1∥∞, ∥x2∥∞) then

$$\delta(\mathbf{H} - \mathbf{x}_1 G + \mathbf{x}_2 G) = \delta(\mathbf{H}) + \mathbf{x}_1 - \mathbf{x}_2.$$

In particular, under the same assumptions δ2(H−x1G+x2G) = δ2(H)⊕x1⊕x2.

### <span id="page-15-0"></span>3.2 Rejection Sampling

Many primitives built from distance functions (e.g. [\[DGI](#page-30-13)+19]) require a large fraction of elements in G to have sufficiently high distance. In light of Lemma [3](#page-15-1) this enables powerful manipulations. However, it also introduces an inversepolynomial failure probability, since a significant fraction of group elements will either be too far (so that distance cannot be measured) or too close. This limitation seems inherent in many applications such as [\[DGI](#page-30-13)+19] where group elements of interest are generated by parties who cannot themselves check their distance. In order to avoid high error rate, we propose a simple rejection sampling procedure. In other words, given a specified distribution on Gn, we draw independent samples from it until one achieves the desired minimum distance. Notably, this is possible for signatures (and not for Trapdoor Hash Functions and related primitives) since the signer holds all secret information and has no limitation in testing the resulting distance.

<span id="page-15-3"></span>In this section we present the main theorem (Theorem [3\)](#page-16-1) detailing the relation between desired distance and distance function parameters. Remarkably, although we adapt from [\[BGI16\]](#page-30-2), their results only consider a fixed group element and make the error probability depend only on the PRF/SUF key choice. In our case instead we must crucially rely on samples being mutually independent and sufficiently spread out over G. To formally capture the last requirement we first introduce the following near collision event.

{16}------------------------------------------------

**Definition 12.** Given a group  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ , random variables  $H_{i,j} \in \mathbb{G}$  for  $i \in \{1, ..., \nu\}$  and  $j \in \{1, ..., n\}$  and an integer  $T \in \mathbb{N}^+$ , we define the near-collision event of **H** for distance T as  $\mathsf{Near}(\mathbf{H}, T) =$ 

$$\exists i_1, i_2 \in \{1, \dots, \nu\}, \ j_1, j_2 \in \{1, \dots, n\}, \ \gamma \in \{0, \dots, T-1\} :$$

$$(i_1, j_1) \neq (i_2, j_2) \land H_{i_1, j_1} = H_{i_2, j_2} + \gamma G.$$

<span id="page-16-1"></span>**Theorem 3.** Let  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ ,  $k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda})$  and  $H_{i,j}$  random variables in  $\mathbb{G}$  distributed independently from k, for  $i \in \{1, \ldots, \nu\}$  and  $j \in \{1, \ldots, n\}$ . For any  $d \in \mathbb{N}^+$  let  $L, T \in \mathbb{N}$  be such that

$$L \ge \log(5nd), \qquad T \ge 2^L \cdot (1 + \ln(n)) + d.$$

If SUF is  $(\nu nT)$ -universal (see Definition 4), then

$$\Pr\left[\nexists i: \delta_{\mathsf{min}}(H_{i,1}, \dots, H_{i,n}) \geq d\right] \leq 2^{-\nu} + \Pr\left[\mathsf{Near}(\mathbf{H}, T)\right].$$

The proof appears in the appendix, Section C.2.

### <span id="page-16-0"></span>3.3 Domain Sampler

Using Theorem 3 we will show that, for a sensible choice of the distance parameters, a specific subset of  $\mathbb{G}^n$  consisting of vectors whose distance is at least d is efficiently sampleable. Concretely, given a matrix  $A \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1,n_2}$ , a vector  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$  and distance parameters  $d\mathbf{p}$ , the set we need to sample from is defined as

$$\mathcal{U}^* := \{ (\mathbf{x}_0, z_0) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q : \delta_{\min} \left( [A\mathbf{x}_0 + z_0\mathbf{u}] \right) \ge d \}.$$

<span id="page-16-2"></span>To do so, we apply a rejection sampling strategy: a sequence of  $\nu$  elements  $(\mathbf{x}_1, z_1), \ldots, (\mathbf{x}_{\nu}, z_{\nu})$  is sampled uniformly from  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q$ , and the first one satisfying  $\delta_{\min}([A\mathbf{x}_i + z_i\mathbf{u}]) \geq d$  is returned. This is formally described in Figure 3. Proofs for the following theorems are deferred to the appendix, Section C.3.

```
 \begin{split} & \frac{\mathsf{Samp}([A]\,, [\mathbf{u}]\,, d, \mathsf{dp})\colon /\!/\; \delta = \delta_{\mathsf{dp}}}{1: & \mathbf{For} \; i \in \{1, \dots, \nu\}\colon \\ & 2: & \mathbf{x}_i \leftarrow^\$ \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2}, \; z_i \leftarrow^\$ \mathbb{F}_q \\ & 3: & \mathbf{If} \; \delta_{\mathsf{min}}\left([A\mathbf{x}_i + z_i\mathbf{u}]\right) \geq d\colon \; \mathbf{Return} \; (\mathbf{x}_i, z_i) \\ & 4: & \mathbf{Return} \perp \quad /\!/\; \mathsf{Give} \; \mathsf{up} \; \mathsf{after} \; \nu \; \mathsf{attempts} \end{split}
```

Fig. 3: Domain sampler with  $A \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1,n_2}$ ,  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$ ,  $d \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $dp = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k, L, T)$ .

<span id="page-16-4"></span>**Theorem 4.** For any  $k \in \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$ ,  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ ,  $[A] \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1, n_2}$ ,  $[\mathbf{u}] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1}$  and any L, T, d integers, let  $(\mathbf{x}, z) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{Samp}([A], [\mathbf{u}], d, \mathsf{dp})$  with  $\mathsf{dp} = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k, L, T)$ . Then conditioning on  $(\mathbf{x}, z) \neq \bot$  we have that  $(\mathbf{x}, z)$  is uniform over

<span id="page-16-3"></span>
$$\mathcal{U}^* = \{ (\mathbf{x}_0, z_0) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q : \ \delta_{\min} \left( [A\mathbf{x}_0 + z_0\mathbf{u}] \right) \ge d \}.$$

{17}------------------------------------------------

**Theorem 5.** For all  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ ,  $[A] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1, n_2}$ ,  $[\mathbf{u}] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1}$  such that  $(0 \neq u_i \neq u_j \ \forall i \neq j)$  and  $d = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , setting

$$L \ge \log(5n_1d), \qquad T \ge 2^L \cdot (1 + \ln(n_1)) + d$$

there exist  $\varepsilon_1(\lambda)$  and a set of SUF keys  $\mathcal{K}_1^* \subseteq \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$ :

1. 
$$\varepsilon_1(\lambda) = \sqrt{\frac{1}{2^{\nu}} + \frac{\nu^2 n_1^2 T}{q}};$$

2. 
$$k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}) \Rightarrow \Pr[k \notin \mathcal{K}_{1}^{*}] \leq \varepsilon_{1}(\lambda);$$

3. 
$$k_0 \in \mathcal{K}_1^*$$
,  $\mathsf{dp} = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k_0, L, T) \Rightarrow \Pr\left[\mathsf{Samp}([A], [\mathbf{u}], d, \mathsf{dp}) = \bot\right] \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda)$ .

Note that the set  $\mathcal{K}_1^*$  depends on all inputs of Samp except the SUF key. While this will suffice for our first construction, the second one will require more flexibility on the set of good SUF keys. The following Theorem shows how to achieve that at the price of mildly worse parameters, when A is of the form  $\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a} - M$ , with M a short matrix. A proof appears in the appendix, Section C.3.

<span id="page-17-1"></span>**Theorem 6.** For all  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ ,  $[\mathbf{u}] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1}$  such that  $(0 \neq u_i \neq u_j \ \forall i \neq j)$  and  $d = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , setting

$$L \ge \log(10n_1d)$$
  $T \ge 2^L \cdot (1 + \ln(n_1)) + 2d$   $\nu \ge 2\lambda$ 

there exists  $\varepsilon_1(\lambda)$  and a set of SUF keys  $\widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^* \subseteq \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$ :

1. 
$$\varepsilon_1(\lambda) = \sqrt{\frac{1}{2^{\nu}} + \frac{\nu^2 n_1^2 T}{q}};$$

2. 
$$k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}) \Rightarrow \Pr\left[k \notin \widehat{\mathcal{K}}_{1}^{*}\right] \leq \varepsilon_{1}(\lambda);$$

3. Setting  $k_0 \in \widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^*$ ,  $dp = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k_0, L, T)$ , for all  $\mathbf{a} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_2}$ , all  $\alpha \in \mathbb{F}_q \setminus \{0\}$ , and all  $M \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1, n_2}$  such that  $\|M\|_{\infty} \leq d$ , then

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Samp}(\left[\mathbf{u}\otimes\mathbf{a}-M\right],\left[\alpha\mathbf{u}\right],d,\mathsf{dp}\right)=\bot\right]\leq\varepsilon_{1}(\lambda).$$

### <span id="page-17-0"></span>3.4 Preimage Sampler

With the key-switching lemma in mind,  $\mathcal{U}^*$  from the previous section represents the domain of one among the two functions we split our (shifted) verification equation into. As informally discussed in the intro, when keys are switched in the security proof, the verification equation takes the following form:

$$\delta_2([A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u} + \mathbf{x}_1 + M_2\mathbf{x}_2]) = \mathbf{t}$$

Where  $A_2 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - M_1$  and  $M_1$  is chosen in the reduction so that for the punctured message(s)  $M_1 \oplus M_2$  is not invertible. Applying Lemma 3, due to  $M_1, M_2, \mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2$  being short, if the group elements have sufficiently large distance this splits into:

$$\delta_2([A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}]) \oplus M_2\mathbf{x}_2 = \mathbf{x}_1 \oplus \mathbf{t}.$$

{18}------------------------------------------------

The goal of this section is to show how to sample  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z)$  in a way that is consistent with Samp, provided  $\mathbf{a}_2$  (i.e. the second key), and  $\mathbf{x}_1$  (which now would be sampled uniformly at random in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ ). We call such procedure PreSamp (detailed in Figure 4) or *preimage sampler* as it returns a uniformly sampled preimage of  $\mathbf{x}_1 \oplus \mathbf{t}$  for

<span id="page-18-0"></span>
$$f_2: \{(\mathbf{x}_2, z) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1} \times \mathbb{F}_q : \delta_{\min}([\mathbf{v} + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}]) \ge d\} \to \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$$
$$f_2(\mathbf{x}_2, z) = \delta_2([\mathbf{v} + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u} + M_2\mathbf{x}_2]).$$

```
\mathsf{PreSamp}([\mathbf{v}], [\mathbf{u}], \mathbf{a}, M_1, M_2, \mathbf{t}, d, \mathsf{dp}):
```

```
1: For i \in \{1, ..., \nu\}:
 2: z_i^* \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q
              // Default to \mathbf{s}_i = \mathbf{0} so line 9 can fail
  3:
              If \delta_{\min}([\mathbf{v} + z_i^*\mathbf{u}]) = \bot: \mathbf{s}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{0}
  4:
              If \delta_{\mathsf{min}}([\mathbf{v} + z_i^*\mathbf{u}]) \neq \bot: \mathbf{s}_i \leftarrow \delta_2([\mathbf{v} + z_i^*\mathbf{u}])
  5:
              S_i := \{ \mathbf{x}_0 \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} : (M_1 \oplus M_2) \mathbf{x}_0 = \mathbf{s}_i \oplus \mathbf{t} \}
  6:
              If S_i = \varnothing: Return \bot
  7:
               \mathbf{x}_i \leftarrow^{\$} S_i
  8:
               If \delta_{\min}([\mathbf{v} + z_i^*\mathbf{u} - M_1\mathbf{x}_i]) \geq d:
  9:
                    // Accept the sample, up to shifting z_i^*
10:
                    Return (\mathbf{x}, z) \leftarrow (\mathbf{x}_i, z_i^* - \mathbf{a}^\top \mathbf{x}_i)
11:
12: Return \perp
```

Fig. 4: Preimage sampler with  $dp = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k, L, T), \mathbf{v}, \mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}, \mathbf{a} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_2}, M_1, M_2 \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}, \mathbf{t} \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1} \text{ and } d \in \mathbb{N}.$ 

The proofs of the following theorems appear in appendix, Section C.4.

<span id="page-18-2"></span>**Theorem 7.** For all  $n_1 \leq n_2$ ,  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ ,  $[\mathbf{u}] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1}$  such that  $(0 \neq u_{j_0} \neq u_{j_1} \ \forall j_0 \neq j_1)$ ,  $\mathbf{a} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_2}$ ,  $d \in \mathbb{N}$ , setting

$$L \ge \log(10n_1 d), \qquad T \ge 2^L \cdot (1 + \ln(n_1)) + 2d, \qquad \nu \ge 2\lambda$$

there exists  $\varepsilon_2(\lambda)$  and a set of SUF keys  $\mathcal{K}_2^* \subseteq \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$ :

<span id="page-18-3"></span>1. 
$$\varepsilon_2(\lambda) = \sqrt{\frac{1}{2^{\nu}} + \frac{\nu^2 n_1^2 T}{q}}$$

- <span id="page-18-4"></span>2.  $k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}) \Rightarrow \Pr[k \notin \mathcal{K}_2^*] \leq \varepsilon_2(\lambda)$
- <span id="page-18-5"></span>3. Setting  $k_0 \in \mathcal{K}_2^*$  and  $dp = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k_0, L, T)$ , then for all  $M_1, M_2 \in \mathbb{N}^{n_1, n_2}$  such that  $M_1 \oplus M_2$  is full rank in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}$  and  $d \geq \max(\|M_1\|_{\infty}, \|M_2\|_{\infty})$  and for all  $\alpha_1, \alpha_2 \in \mathbb{F}_q$  (with  $\alpha_2 \neq 0$ ),  $\mathbf{t} \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{PreSamp}(\left[\alpha_{1}\mathbf{u}\right],\left[\alpha_{2}\mathbf{u}\right],\mathbf{a},M_{1},M_{2},\mathbf{t},d,\mathsf{dp}\right)=\bot\right]\leq\varepsilon_{2}(\lambda).$$

{19}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-19-3"></span>**Theorem 8.** For any  $n_1 \leq n_2$ ,  $k \in \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$ ,  $(\mathbb{G}, G, q) \in \mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ ,  $[\mathbf{v}]$ ,  $[\mathbf{u}] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1}$ ,  $\mathbf{a} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_2}$ ,  $M_1, M_2 \in \mathbb{N}^{n_1, n_2}$  such that  $M_1 \oplus M_2$  is full rank modulo 2,  $\mathbf{t} \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ ,  $L, T = \text{poly}(\lambda)$  and  $d \geq \max(\|M_1\|_{\infty}, \|M_2\|_{\infty})$ , let  $(\mathbf{x}, z) \leftarrow^{\$} \text{PreSamp}([\mathbf{v}], [\mathbf{u}], \mathbf{a}, M_1, M_2, \mathbf{t}, d, \mathsf{dp})$ . Then conditioning on  $(\mathbf{x}, z) \neq \bot$  we have that  $(\mathbf{x}, z)$  is uniform over

$$\left\{ (\mathbf{x}_0, z_0) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q \mid \begin{array}{l} \delta_{\min}([\mathbf{v} + A\mathbf{x}_0 + z_0\mathbf{u}]) \ge d \\ \delta_2([\mathbf{v} + A\mathbf{x}_0 + z_0\mathbf{u} + M_2\mathbf{x}_0]) = \mathbf{t} \end{array} \right\}$$

where  $A = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a} - M_1 \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1, n_2}$ .

# <span id="page-19-0"></span>4 Selectively Secure Short Signatures from DDH

### <span id="page-19-1"></span>4.1 Construction

We are now ready to present our selectively secure construction based on DDH. The scheme, detailed in Figure 5, is based on a family of functions  $F_m$  (defined by a set of public parameters in the verification key) such that

- $F_m$  is preimage sampleable for all but one m;
- public parameters can be generated so that for a secret  $m^*$ ,  $F_{m^*}$  has image size *sparse* in its domain.

We abuse terminology and refer to the second property as  $F_{m^*}$  being  $lossy^{12}$  Given such  $F_m$  and a target output  $\mathbf{t}$  in  $\mathsf{vk}$ , a signature for m is a preimage of  $\mathbf{t}$  for  $F_m$ . The verifier simply checks  $F_m(\sigma) = \mathbf{t}$ . Security is argued by programming  $F_{m^*}$  to be lossy, with  $m^*$  being the message selected by the adversary, so that w.h.p. no preimage for  $\mathbf{t}$  exists. More in details, the function  $F_m$  is determined by: a group  $\mathbb{G}$ ; a vector  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$ ; two matrices with large entries  $A_1, A_2 \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1, n_2}$  of the form  $A_i = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_i$ ; two binary matrices  $B_1, B_2$  acting as UHF (see Section 2.3); a distance function  $\delta$ . Given these  $F_m : \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1} \times \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q \to \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$  is defined as

$$F_m(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) = \delta_2 \left( \left[ A_1 \mathbf{x}_1 + A_2 \mathbf{x}_2 + z \mathbf{u} + \mathbf{x}_1 + M \mathbf{x}_2 \right] \right)$$

where  $M = B_1 \oplus (P_m B_2)$ , see Section 2.5 for the definition of  $P_m$ . All the parameters are specified in Table 2.

Preimage sampling. In order to find a preimage for a vector  $\mathbf{t}$  we rely on the homomorphic properties of  $\delta_2$  and the fact that  $[A_1]$ ,  $[A_2]$ ,  $[\mathbf{u}]$  defines a chameleon hash. The goal will be to disentangle  $\mathbf{x}_1$  from  $\mathbf{x}_2, z$ . To do so, let us restrict the domain of  $F_m$  to those inputs for which  $A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}$  has large enough minimum distance w.r.t.  $\delta$ . Then  $F_m$  can be rewritten as

$$F_m(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) = \delta_2 ([A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}]) \oplus M\mathbf{x}_2 \oplus \mathbf{x}_1$$

<span id="page-19-2"></span>For all m in fact  $F_m$  is an (almost) surjective function which already loses some information. However  $F_{m^*}$  crucially loses  $\Theta(\lambda)$  additional bits.

{20}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-20-1"></span>

| Par.      | Value                                 | Description                            |
|-----------|---------------------------------------|----------------------------------------|
| $\lambda$ | _                                     | security parameter                     |
| $\mu$     | $2\lambda$                            | supported message length               |
| q         | _                                     | group order                            |
| $n_1$     | $\lambda + \log q$                    | first trapdoor bit length              |
| $n_2$     | $5\lambda + 2\log q + \log(4\lambda)$ | second trapdoor bit length             |
| $\nu$     | $2\lambda$                            | rejection-sampling repetitions         |
| L         | $\log(10n_1d)$                        | SUF output length for $\delta$         |
| T         | $10n_1d \cdot (1 + \ln(n_1)) + 2d$    | maximum supported distance in $\delta$ |
| d         | $n_2 + 1$                             | distance                               |

Table 2: parameters for the *selectively* secure scheme.

Next, knowing  $\mathbf{a}_1$ , we can consider the following domain shift  $z^* = z + \mathbf{a}_1^{\top} \mathbf{x}_1$ . Calling  $F_m^*$  the function

$$F_m^*(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) = \delta_2([A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}]) \oplus M\mathbf{x}_2 \oplus \mathbf{x}_1,$$

we can see that  $F_m^*(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = F_m(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z)$ . In order to find a preimage for  $\mathbf{t}$  then it suffices to sample  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*)$  with a large enough minimum distance through Samp (see Section 3.3), find the right  $\mathbf{x}_1$  that makes  $F_m^*(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = \mathbf{t}$ , and finally undo the shift by setting  $z = z^* - \mathbf{a}_1^{\top} \mathbf{x}_1$ .

Lossier mode in one point. Given  $m^*$ , we can program the public parameters so that  $F_{m^*}$  is lossier. To do so, we set  $A_1 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1 - I$  and  $A_2 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - M^*$  where  $M^* = B_1 \oplus (P_{m^*}B_2)$ . This parameter choice gives us that  $F_{m^*}(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) =$ 

$$= \delta_2 \left( \left[ \left( \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1 \cdot \mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 \cdot \mathbf{x}_2 + z \mathbf{u} \right) \right] \right)$$
  
=  $\delta_2 \left( \left[ \left( \mathbf{a}_1^\top \mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{a}_2^\top \mathbf{x}_2 + z \right) \cdot \mathbf{u} \right] \right)$ 

which has image size at most q. Setting  $\mathbf{t} \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$  to have length  $\log q + \lambda$ , the image of  $F_{m^*}$  is indeed sparse. Showing how to simulate signatures with this new key relies on a combination of DDH hybrids to adapt each matrix and the Key Switching Lemma (Lemma 1), we defer the details to the proof of Theorem 10.

### <span id="page-20-0"></span>4.2 Correctness

<span id="page-20-2"></span>**Theorem 9.** If SUF is  $(\nu n_1 T)$ -universal, then the signature scheme in Figure 5 with parameters as in Table 2 is correct.

*Proof.* The proof consists of three parts: (1) use the results in Section 3.4 to identify a set of good SUF keys; (2) show that a signature is accepting unless  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = \bot$  in the signing procedure, line 4; (3) prove the latter event occurs with negligible probability that is independent from the given message.

{21}------------------------------------------------

```
\mathsf{S}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}):
                                                                                              S.Sign(sk, m): // m \in \mathbb{F}_2^{\mu}
                                                                                               1: Set dp = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k, L, T), \delta = \delta_{dp}.
 1: (\mathbb{G}, G, q) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{G.Gen}(1^{\lambda})
                                                                                               2: Set M = B_1 \oplus (P_m B_2) // \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}
 2: \mathbf{u} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}
                                                                                               3: (\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{Samp}([A_2], [\mathbf{u}], d, \mathsf{dp})
 3: \mathbf{a}_1 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}, \, \mathbf{a}_2 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_2}
                                                                                                4: If (\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = \bot: Return \bot
 4: A_i \leftarrow \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_i
                                                                                               5: \mathbf{x}_1 \leftarrow \delta_2([A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u}]) \oplus M\mathbf{x}_2 \oplus \mathbf{t}
 5: \mathbf{t} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}
 6: B_1, B_2 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}
                                                                                               6: \quad z \leftarrow z^* - \mathbf{a}_1^\top \mathbf{x}_1
                                                                                                7: Return \sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z)
 7: k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda})
 8: vk = ([A_1], [A_2], [\mathbf{u}], \mathbf{t}, B_1, B_2, k, \mathbb{G})
 9: sk = (vk, a_1)
10: Return (vk, sk)
S.Vfy(vk, m, \sigma):
 1: Set dp = (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k, L, T), \delta = \delta_{dp} and M = B_1 \oplus (P_m B_2)
 2: Parse \sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z) // \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1} \times \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q
 3: b \leftarrow (\delta_2([A_1\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u} + \mathbf{x}_1 + M\mathbf{x}_2]) == \mathbf{t})
 4: Return b
```

Fig. 5: Selectively secure signature from DDH.  $f_k$  is  $(\nu n_1 T)$ -universal.

Regarding the first step, as per Table 2 we have  $L > \log(5n_1d)$  and  $T > 2^L \cdot (1 + \ln(n_1)) + d$ . Given  $(\mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda})$ , up to negligible probability we have  $0 \neq u_i \neq u_j$  for all  $i \neq j$ . Therefore, conditioning on  $\mathbf{u}$  satisfying the above conditions, there exist a negligible  $\varepsilon_1(\lambda)$  and a set  $\mathcal{K}_1^* \subseteq \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$  satisfying the three conditions of Theorem 5. Next we observe that for any  $m \in \mathcal{M}_{\lambda}$  and  $\sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S}.\mathsf{Sign}(\mathsf{sk}, m)$ , if  $\sigma \neq \bot$  then the verifier accepts. Indeed by Theorem 4  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) \neq \bot$  implies that  $\delta([A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u}]) \geq d$ . Calling  $\sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z)$  and  $M = B_1 \oplus P_m B_2$  then we get by construction that

$$\delta_{2}([A_{1}\mathbf{x}_{1} + A_{2}\mathbf{x}_{2} + z\mathbf{u} + M\mathbf{x}_{2} + \mathbf{x}_{1}]) = \delta_{2}([(\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_{1})\mathbf{x}_{1} + A_{2}\mathbf{x}_{2} + z\mathbf{u} + M\mathbf{x}_{2}])$$

$$= \delta_{2}([A_{2}\mathbf{x}_{2} + (\mathbf{a}_{1}^{\top}\mathbf{x}_{1} + z)\mathbf{u} + M\mathbf{x}_{2} + \mathbf{x}_{1}])$$

$$= \delta_{2}([A_{2}\mathbf{x}_{2} + z^{*}\mathbf{u} + M\mathbf{x}_{2} + \mathbf{x}_{1}])$$

$$= \delta_{2}([A_{2}\mathbf{x}_{2} + z^{*}\mathbf{u}]) \oplus M\mathbf{x}_{2} \oplus \mathbf{x}_{1} = \mathbf{t}$$

where the first equality follows as  $A_1 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1$ , the second by definition of  $\otimes$  (see Section 2.1), the third by the definition of z. Fourth equality is by Lemma 3 as  $\|M\mathbf{x}_2 + \mathbf{x}_1\|_{\infty} \leq n_2 + 1 = d$ . The last follows by construction of  $\mathbf{x}_1$ . Hence S.Vfy(vk,  $\sigma$ , m)  $\to 1$  if  $\sigma \neq \bot$ . For ease of notation, let Good be the set of "good" vectors  $\mathbf{u}$ , i.e. the vectors satisfying  $u_i \neq 0$  for all i and  $u_i \neq u_j$  for all  $i \neq j$ . We can bound  $\Pr[\mathsf{S.Vfy}(\mathsf{vk}, \sigma, m) = 0] \leq \Pr[\sigma = \bot] \leq$ 

$$\leq \sum_{\bar{A}_2} \sum_{\bar{\mathbf{u}} \in \mathsf{Good}} \Pr\left[\sigma = \bot \, | A_2 = \bar{A}_2, \mathbf{u} = \bar{\mathbf{u}}\right] \Pr\left[A_2 = \bar{A}_2, \mathbf{u} = \bar{\mathbf{u}}\right] + \Pr\left[\mathbf{u} \notin \mathsf{Good}\right].$$

{22}------------------------------------------------

Due to Theorem 5, for fixed  $\bar{A}_2$  and  $\bar{\mathbf{u}}$  we bound  $\Pr\left[\sigma = \bot \, | A_2 = \bar{A}_2, \mathbf{u} = \bar{\mathbf{u}}\right] \le$ 

$$\Pr\left[\sigma = \bot \mid A_2 = \bar{A}_2, \mathbf{u} = \bar{\mathbf{u}}, k \in \mathcal{K}_1^*\right] + \Pr\left[k \notin \mathcal{K}_1^*\right] \leq 2\varepsilon_1(\lambda)$$

whereas  $\Pr[\mathbf{u} \notin \mathsf{Good}]$  can be bounded above by  $\frac{n_1(n_1+1)}{2q}$ . Considering that  $\varepsilon_1(\lambda)$  does not depend on  $\bar{A}_2$  or  $\bar{\mathbf{u}}$  can thus bound the probability of failure by

$$2\varepsilon_1(\lambda) + n_1(n_1+1)/2q.$$

## <span id="page-22-0"></span>4.3 Unforgeability

<span id="page-22-1"></span>**Theorem 10.** If DDH is hard for G.Gen and SUF is  $(\nu n_1 T)$ -universal, then the signature scheme in Figure 5 with parameters as in Table 2 is selectively unforgeable under chosen message attacks.

Proof. We prove the theorem through a sequence of hybrids. Informally, we can break up the proof into four main steps, calling  $m^*$  the message our selective adversary tries to forge. Initially, using  $\mathbf{a}_1$  trapdoor for  $A_1$  to sign, we puncture  $A_2$  in  $m^*$ . Next, we switch to a signing procedure that relies on  $\mathbf{a}_2$  trapdoor for  $A_2$  to sign all messages but  $m^*$ . Third, we modify  $A_1$  so that no message can be signed using its trapdoor (while all but  $m^*$  can still be signed with  $A_2$ ). Finally, we show that up to negligible probability there exists no valid signature for  $m^*$  in the last hybrid. For the two transformation of  $A_2$  and  $A_1$  we make use of MDDH along with Lemma 9 and 10.

Formally, given a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we define the following hybrids:

- H<sub>1</sub>: Real SUF-CMA game;
- $H_2$ : As  $H_1$ , but sample  $A_2$  uniformly in  $\mathbb{F}_q^{n_1,n_2}$ ;
- $\mathsf{H}_3$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_2$ , but, after  $m^* \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda})$ , set in the signature's verification key  $A_2 \leftarrow \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 M^*$  with  $M^* = B_1 + P_{m^*}B_2$ ;
- $\mathsf{H}_4$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_3$ , but  $\mathsf{sk}^* = (\mathsf{vk}, \mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{a}_2)$  and  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{sk}^*, \mathcal{Q}}(m)$  returns  $\mathsf{S.Sign}((\mathsf{vk}, \mathbf{a}_1), m)$ ;
- $\mathsf{H}_5$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_4$ , but  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{sk}^*,Q}(m)$  returns  $\sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S.Sign2}(\mathsf{sk}^*,m^*,m)$  as in Figure 6;
- $H_6$ : As  $H_5$ , but sample  $A_1$  uniformly in  $\mathbb{F}_q^{n_1,n_1}$ ;
- $H_7$ : As  $H_6$ , but set  $A_1 \leftarrow \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1 I$ ;

In the following we will show  $H_1$ ,  $H_2$ ,  $H_3$  are computationally indistinguishable under the MDDH assumption (Definition 7),  $H_3$ ,  $H_4$  are identical up to syntactical changes,  $H_4$ ,  $H_5$  are statistically indistinguishable, while  $H_5$ ,  $H_6$ ,  $H_7$  are indistinguishable again under the MDDH assumption. Finally we will prove that up to negligible probability, there exists no valid signature for  $m^*$  in  $H_7$ .

{23}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-23-0"></span>S.Sign2(sk\*, $m^*$ , m): 1: Parse sk\* = (vk, $\mathbf{a}_1$ , $\mathbf{a}_2$ ) 2: Parse vk = ([ $A_1$ ], [ $A_2$ ], [ $\mathbf{u}$ ], $\mathbf{t}$ , $B_1$ , $B_2$ , k, $\mathbb{G}$ ) 3: dp $\leftarrow$ ( $\mathbb{G}$ , G, q, k, L, T) // deriving G, q from $\mathbb{G}$ 4: $\mathbf{x}_1 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ 5: $\mathbf{v} \leftarrow A_1 \mathbf{x}_1$ // $\in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$ 6: $M^* \leftarrow B_1 \oplus P_m * B_2$ // $\in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}$ 7: $M \leftarrow B_1 \oplus P_m B_2$ // $\in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}$ 8: $(\mathbf{x}_2, z) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{PreSamp}([\mathbf{v}], [\mathbf{u}], \mathbf{a}_2, M^*, M, \mathbf{t} \oplus \mathbf{x}_1, d, \mathsf{dp})$ 9: Return $\sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z)$

Fig. 6: Alternative signature scheme using  $\mathbf{a}_2$  to sign any  $m \neq m^*$ .

Indistinguishability of  $H_1$ ,  $H_2$ . Let  $\mathcal{D}$  be a distinguisher for  $H_1$ ,  $H_2$ . In Figure 7 we describe a reduction  $\mathcal{B}_1(\mathbb{G}, [C])$  to  $(n_1, n_2 + 1)$ -MDDH. Initially, it executes  $\mathcal{D}$  and waits for  $m^*$ . Next, in order to generate  $\mathsf{vk}$ ,  $\mathsf{sk}$ , it sets  $[\mathbf{u}]$  as the first column of [C] and  $[A_2]$  as the remaining  $n_2$  columns.  $[A_1]$  is set as  $[\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1]$ , which can be computed given only  $[\mathbf{u}]$  and sampling  $\mathbf{a}_1$ . The remaining public key entries are sampled as per S.Gen, and signing queries are answered via  $\mathsf{S.Sign}((\mathsf{vk}, \mathbf{a}_1), m)$ . Note neither  $\mathbf{u}$  nor  $\mathbf{a}_2$  are required to sign. Finally it returns the same bit returned by  $\mathcal{D}$ .

```
\mathcal{B}_{1}(\mathbb{G}, [C]): \ /\!/ \ [C] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_{1}, n_{2}+1}
1: \quad \text{Run } m^{*} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{D}(1^{\lambda})
2: \quad \text{Derive } (\mathbb{G}, G, q) \text{ from } \mathbb{G} \text{ and parse } [C] = [\mathbf{u}], [A_{2}] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_{1}} \times \mathbb{G}^{n_{1}, n_{2}}
3: \quad \mathbf{a}_{1} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_{q}^{n_{1}}, [A_{1}] \leftarrow [\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_{1}], \mathbf{t} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_{2}^{n_{1}}, B_{1}, B_{2} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_{2}^{n_{1}, n_{2}} \text{ and } k \leftarrow^{\$} \text{SUF.Gen}(1^{\lambda})
4: \quad \text{vk} = ([A_{1}], [A_{2}], [\mathbf{u}], \mathbf{t}, B_{1}, B_{2}, k, \mathbb{G}) \text{ and sk} = (\text{vk}, \mathbf{a}_{1})
5: \quad \text{Run } \mathcal{D}(1^{\lambda}, \text{vk})
6: \quad \mathbf{When } \mathcal{D} \text{ queries } m \in \{0, 1\}^{\mu}: \quad \text{Send } \sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \text{S.Sign}(\text{sk}, m) \text{ to } \mathcal{D}
7: \quad \mathbf{When } \mathcal{D} \text{ returns } b: \quad \mathbf{Return } b
```

Fig. 7: Reduction of the indistinguishability of  $H_1$ ,  $H_2$  to  $(n_1, n_2 + 1)$ -MDDH.

Using Lemma 9 and 10, it suffices to study the advantage of  $\mathcal{B}_1$  in distinguishing a uniform  $C_0$  from  $C_1$  of the form  $\mathbf{u} \otimes (1, \mathbf{v})$  with uniform  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$ ,  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_2}$ . First, when  $C = C_0$  is uniform, so is  $\mathbf{u}$  and  $A_2$  as in  $H_2$ . The remaining components of the verification key and secret key  $\mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{t}, B_1, B_2, k$  are sampled correctly and independently from  $\mathbf{u}, A_2$ , and  $A_1 = \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1$ . Hence

$$\Pr\left[1 \leftarrow \mathcal{B}_1(\mathbb{G}, [C_0])\right] = \Pr\left[1 \leftarrow \mathcal{D}^{\mathsf{H}_2}(1^{\lambda})\right].$$

{24}------------------------------------------------

Conversely, when  $C = C_1$  then  $C = (\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2)$  for uniformly sampled  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{a}_2$ . In particular  $[\mathbf{u}]$  and  $[A_2] = [\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2]$  are correctly distributed as in  $\mathsf{H}_1$ . As before, the remaining public and secret key components, as well as signature queries, are also correctly sampled. Thus  $\Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathcal{B}_1(\mathbb{G}, [C_1])] = \Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathcal{D}^{\mathsf{H}_1}(1^{\lambda})]$  implies that  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_1,\mathsf{H}_2}(\lambda) = \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{B}_1}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

Indistinguishability of  $H_2$ ,  $H_3$ . We again reduce a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$  to an adversary  $\mathcal{B}_2$  for  $(n_1, n_2 + 1)$ -MDDH. Initially it gets  $m^*$  the challenge message from  $\mathcal{D}(1^{\lambda})$ . Then, it samples all public and secret key component  $\mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{t}, B_1, B_2, k$  with the exception of  $[\mathbf{u}], [A_1], [A_2]$ . To complete  $\mathsf{vk}$ , given  $[C] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1, n_2 + 1}$ , it separates the first column from the rest, i.e. parsing  $C = \mathbf{c}_0 || C^*$  with  $\mathbf{c}_0 \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$  and  $C^* \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1, n_2}$ . The first column is set to  $[\mathbf{u}] = [\mathbf{c}_0]$ , while  $[A_2] = [C^* - M^*]$  with  $M^* = B_1 \oplus (P_{m^*}B_2)$ . Finally  $[A_1] = [\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1]$ . Signature queries are then answered with  $\mathsf{sk} = (\mathsf{vk}, \mathbf{a}_1)$  as prescribed in both games.

```
\mathcal{B}_{2}(\mathbb{G},[C]): \ /\!/ \ [C] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_{1},n_{2}+1}
1: \quad \text{Run } m^{*} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{D}(1^{\lambda})
2: \quad \text{Derive } (\mathbb{G},G,q) \text{ from } \mathbb{G}
3: \quad \mathbf{t} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_{2}^{n_{1}}, \ B_{1}, B_{2} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_{2}^{n_{1},n_{2}} \text{ and } k \leftarrow^{\$} \text{SUF.Gen}(1^{\lambda}), \ M^{*} \leftarrow B_{1} \oplus (P_{m^{*}}B_{2})
4: \quad \text{Parse } [C] = [\mathbf{c_{0}}], [C^{*}] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_{1}} \times \mathbb{G}^{n_{1},n_{2}}. \text{ Set } [\mathbf{u}] = [\mathbf{c_{0}}] \text{ and } [A_{2}] = [C^{*} - M^{*}]
5: \quad \mathbf{a_{1}} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_{q}^{n_{1}}, \ [A_{1}] \leftarrow [\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a_{1}}]
6: \quad \text{vk} = ([A_{1}], [A_{2}], [\mathbf{u}], \mathbf{t}, B_{1}, B_{2}, k, \mathbb{G}) \text{ and sk} = (\text{vk}, \mathbf{a_{1}})
7: \quad \text{Run } \mathcal{D}(1^{\lambda}, \text{vk})
8: \quad \mathbf{When } \mathcal{D} \text{ queries } m \in \{0, 1\}^{\mu}: \quad \text{Send } \sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \text{ S.Sign}(\text{sk}, m) \text{ to } \mathcal{D}
9: \quad \mathbf{When } \mathcal{D} \text{ returns } b: \quad \mathbf{Return } b
```

Fig. 8: Reduction of the indistinguishability of  $H_2$ ,  $H_3$  to  $(n_1, n_2 + 1)$ -MDDH.

It is easy to see that in both game,  $\mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{t}, B_1, B_2, k$ ,  $\mathbb{G}$  are identically distributed, and  $[A_1] = [\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1]$  is computed as prescribed. Using Lemma 9 and 10 it suffices to study the advantage with which  $\mathcal{B}_2$  distinguishes a uniform  $C_0$  from  $C_1 = (\mathbf{u} \otimes (1, \mathbf{v}))$  for uniform  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v}$ . When  $C = C_0$  is uniformly distributed, then so are both  $\mathbf{c}_0$  and  $C^*$ . In particular  $\mathbf{u}, A_2 = C^* - M^*$  are uniformly distributed (note  $C_0$  and  $(m^*, B_1, B_2)$  are independently distributed). Thus  $\mathcal{B}_1$  perfectly simulates  $H_2$ . Conversely, when  $C = C_1 = (\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2)$  we have that  $[\mathbf{u}]$  and  $[A_2] = (\mathbf{u} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2) - M^*$  are computed as prescribed. Thus  $\mathcal{B}_2$  perfectly simulates  $H_3$  in this case. In particular

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_2,\mathsf{H}_3}_{\mathcal{D}}(\lambda) \ \leq \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{B}_2}(\lambda) \ \leq \ \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Indistinguishability of H<sub>3</sub>, H<sub>4</sub>. The change is only syntactic as both vk and the signatures distribution are not affected by the larger secret key.

{25}------------------------------------------------

Indistinguishability of  $H_4$ ,  $H_5$ . Let  $\mathcal{D}$  be a possibly unbounded distinguisher for the two games. Without loss of generality, we assume it to always make exactly  $\vartheta = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  signature queries. The final bit it returns interacting with either  $H_4$  or  $H_5$  is a deterministic function of its view, that is respectively

$$\mathsf{H}_{4} : \left(\rho, \left[A_{1}\right], \left[A_{2}\right], \left[\mathbf{u}\right], \mathbf{t}, B_{1}, B_{2}, k, \mathbb{G}, \sigma_{1}, \ldots, \sigma_{\vartheta}\right),$$

$$\mathsf{H}_{5} : \left(\widehat{\rho}, \left[\widehat{A}_{1}\right], \left[\widehat{A}_{2}\right], \left[\widehat{\mathbf{u}}\right], \widehat{\mathbf{t}}, \widehat{B}_{1}, \widehat{B}_{2}, \widehat{k}, \widehat{\mathbb{G}}, \widehat{\sigma}_{1}, \ldots, \widehat{\sigma}_{\vartheta}\right)$$

where  $\rho$ ,  $\widehat{\rho}$  are  $\mathcal{D}$ 's random coins. To show  $\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_4,\mathsf{H}_5}_{\mathcal{D}}(\lambda)$  is negligible, it then suffices to show that the two views have negligible statistical distance. To further simplify our task we study the vectors
