$$\mathbf{x}_1 = \delta_2([A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u}]) \oplus M\mathbf{x}_2 \oplus \mathbf{t} = \delta_2([A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u} + M\mathbf{x}_2]) \oplus \mathbf{t}$$
  
=  $f_1^{-1}(f_2(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*)) = f_1^{-1}(f_2(\mathbf{y}_2, w^*)) = \mathbf{y}_1$ 

where the second equality follows by Corollary 1 since  $\delta_{\min}([A_2\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u}]) \geq d \geq n_2 \geq \|M\mathbf{x}_2\|_{\infty}$ . Hence  $\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1$  have the same constant distribution. By Lemma 4 then

$$\Delta((\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z^*), (\mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{y}_2, w^*)) \leq \Delta((\mathbf{x}_2, z^*), (\mathbf{y}_2, w^*)) \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda).$$

Proof of Claim 13. Recall  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z})$  is the output of S.Sign2 in Figure 6. By construction  $\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{y}}_1$  are uniformly sampled over  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ . Hence they have zero statistical distance. Next, conditioning on  $\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 = \widehat{\mathbf{y}}_1$ , we study the distance of  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*)$  and  $(\widehat{\mathbf{y}}_2, \widehat{w}^*)$ .

Let bad the event  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}) = \bot$ . In order to apply Theorem 7

- 1.  $\widehat{B}_2 \in \mathcal{B}_1$  implies  $\widehat{B}_2$  is full rank. Since  $\widehat{m} \neq \widehat{m}^*$ , then  $P_{m-m^*}$  is invertible (Theorem 2) and therefore  $\widehat{M}^* \oplus \widehat{M} = P_{\widehat{m}-\widehat{m}^*}\widehat{B}_2$  is full rank.
- 2.  $\widehat{A}_1\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 = (\widehat{\mathbf{u}} \otimes \widehat{\mathbf{a}}_1)\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 = (\widehat{\mathbf{a}}_1^{\top}\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1) \cdot \widehat{\mathbf{u}}$ . Hence the first input of PreSamp is parallel to  $[\widehat{\mathbf{u}}]$ .
- 3.  $d \ge n_2 \ge \|\widehat{M}^*\|_{\infty}$  and  $d \ge n_2 \ge \|\widehat{M}\|_{\infty}$ .

Hence,  $\hat{k} = k_0 \in \mathcal{K}_2^*$  implies  $\Pr[\mathsf{bad}] \leq \varepsilon_2(\lambda)$ . Furthermore by Theorem 8 we have that  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z})_{|\neg\mathsf{bad}}$  is uniform over

$$\left\{ (\mathbf{x}_0, z_0) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q \; \middle| \; \begin{array}{l} \delta_{\min} \left( \left[ \widehat{A}_1 \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 + \widehat{A}_2 \mathbf{x}_0 + z_0 \widehat{\mathbf{u}} \right] \right) \geq d \\ \delta_2 \left( \left[ \widehat{A}_1 \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 + \widehat{A}_2 \mathbf{x}_0 + z_0 \widehat{\mathbf{u}} + \widehat{M} \mathbf{x}_0 \right] \right) = \widehat{\mathbf{t}} \oplus \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 \end{array} \right\}.$$

Recalling that we defined  $\widehat{z}^* = \widehat{z} - \widehat{\mathbf{a}}_1^{\top} \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1$ , and that  $\widehat{\mathbf{t}} \oplus \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 = f_1(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1)$  we have that  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*)_{|\neg \mathsf{bad}}$  is uniform over

$$\left\{ (\mathbf{x}_0, z_0^*) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q \; \middle| \; \begin{array}{l} \delta_{\min} \left( \left[ \widehat{A}_2 \mathbf{x}_0 + z_0^* \widehat{\mathbf{u}} \right] \right) \geq d \\ \delta_2 \left( \widehat{A}_2 \mathbf{x}_0 + z_0^* \widehat{\mathbf{u}} + \widehat{M} \mathbf{x}_0 \right) \; = \; f_1(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1) \end{array} \right\}.$$

{53}------------------------------------------------

Notice the first condition means  $(\mathbf{x}_0, z_0^*) \in S^*$ , while the second one is equivalent to  $f_2(\mathbf{x}_0, z_0^*) = f_1(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1)$ . Therefore  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*)_{|\neg \mathsf{bad}}$  is uniform over  $f_2^{-1}(f_1(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1))$  and in particular it follows the same distribution of  $(\widehat{\mathbf{y}}_2, \widehat{w}^*)$ . In conclusion

$$\Delta((\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*), (\widehat{\mathbf{y}}_2, \widehat{w}^*)) \leq \Pr[\mathsf{bad}] \leq \varepsilon_2(\lambda)$$

which, by Lemma 4, implies  $\Delta((\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*), (\widehat{\mathbf{y}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{y}}_2, \widehat{w}^*)) \leq \varepsilon_2(\lambda)$ .

## <span id="page-53-0"></span>D.2 Adaptively Secure Scheme: Correctness

Proof of Theorem 11. The proof is analogous to that of Theorem 9. The main differences are that S.Vfy further checks the minimum distance, and that Samp's input depends on the message being signed. In order to bound the probability of  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = \bot$ , we will rely on Theorem 6 (as opposed to Theorem 5).

Regarding the first step, as per Table 3 we have  $L > \log(10n_1d)$ ,  $T > 2^L \cdot (1 + \ln(n_1)) + 2d$ . Given  $(\mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda})$ , up to negligible probability we have  $0 \neq u_i \neq u_j$  for all  $i \neq j$ . For any  $m \in \mathbb{F}_2^{\mu}$  and  $\sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S}.\mathsf{Sign}(\mathsf{sk}, m)$ , let us call  $\alpha = \alpha_0 + m_1\alpha_1 + \ldots + m_{\mu}\alpha_{\mu}$ . Then  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)} = \alpha \mathbf{u}$ ,  $A_1^{(m)} = \mathbf{u} \otimes (\alpha \cdot \mathbf{a}_1)$  and  $A_2^{(m)} = \mathbf{u} \otimes (\alpha \cdot \mathbf{a}_2)$ . If  $\alpha = 0$ , then the verifier always accepts. If  $\alpha \neq 0$ , conditioning on  $\mathbf{u}$  satisfying the above conditions, also  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)}$  satisfies the same conditions; hence there exist a negligible  $\varepsilon_1(\lambda)$  and a set  $\widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^* \subseteq \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$  satisfying the three conditions of Theorem 6.

Next we observe that, if  $\sigma \neq \bot$ , then the verifier accepts. By Theorem 4,  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) \neq \bot$  implies that  $\delta_{\min}([A_2^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_2 + z^*\mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) \geq d$  and so the first condition of the verifier, namely

$$\delta_{\min}([A_1^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}^{(m)} + \mathbf{x}_1]) \ge d,$$

is verified, since by construction  $z = \mathbf{a}_1^{\top} \mathbf{x}_1 - z^*$ . Regarding the second check we have that, calling  $\sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z)$ 

$$\delta_{2}\left(\left[A_{1}^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_{1}+A_{2}^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_{2}+z\mathbf{u}^{(m)}+\mathbf{x}_{1}\right]\right) = \delta_{2}\left(\left[A_{2}^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_{2}+z^{*}\mathbf{u}^{(m)}+\mathbf{x}_{1}\right]\right)$$

$$=\delta_{2}\left(\left[A_{2}^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_{2}+z^{*}\mathbf{u}^{(m)}\right]\right) \oplus \mathbf{x}_{1} = \mathbf{t}$$

where the first equality follows because  $z = \mathbf{a}_1^{\top} \mathbf{x}_1 - z^*$  and  $A_1^{(m)} = \mathbf{u}^{(m)} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1$ . The second one is by Lemma 3 as  $\|\mathbf{x}_1\|_{\infty} \leq 1 \leq n_2 + 1 = d$ . The last follows by construction of  $\mathbf{x}_1$ . Hence S.Vfy(vk,  $\sigma$ , m)  $\to 1$  if  $\sigma \neq \bot$ .

Finally, to show  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = \bot$  with negligible probability, we use the third implication of Theorem 6 (setting M as the zero matrix) because the image of  $A_2^{(m)}$  is in the linear span  $\mathbf{u}$ . Hence  $\Pr\left[\mathsf{Samp}\left(\left[A_2^{(m)}\right],\left[\mathbf{u}^{(m)}\right],d,\mathsf{dp}\right)=\bot\right] \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda)$ , which allows us to conclude that

$$\begin{split} \Pr\left[\mathsf{S.Vfy}(\mathsf{vk},\sigma,m) = 0\right] & \leq \ \Pr\left[\sigma = \bot \, | \alpha = 0\right] + \Pr\left[\sigma = \bot \, | \alpha \neq 0\right] \\ & \leq \ 2\varepsilon_1(\lambda) + \frac{n_1(n_1+1)}{2q}. \end{split}$$

{54}------------------------------------------------

Notice that in the last inequality we used the fact that if  $\alpha = 0$  then  $\Pr[\sigma = \bot] =$ 0 and we bounded the second term conditioning on the value of  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)}$  (in the same way as we did in Theorem 9) and considering the remarks in the first step of this proof. 

### <span id="page-54-0"></span>D.3Adaptively Secure Scheme: Unforgeability

*Proof of Theorem 12.* The proof combines techniques used in the proof of Theorem 2 with a partitioning strategy. We proceed through a sequence of hybrids against an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  which, without loss of generality, makes exactly  $\vartheta = \vartheta(\lambda) = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  queries.

- H<sub>0</sub>: Real EUF-CMA game (see Figure 1).
- $\mathsf{H}_1$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_0$ , but initially sample  $h \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell,\mu}$  and  $y \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell}$  where  $\ell = \log(2\vartheta)$ . At the end of the execution, let  $m_1, \ldots, m_{\vartheta}$  the queried message and  $(m^*, \sigma^*)$ the final forgery. Return 1 iff the winning condition in  $H_1$  is satisfied<sup>15</sup> and

$$h(m_1) \neq y, \ldots, h(m_{\vartheta}) \neq y, h(m^*) = y.$$

- $H_2$ : As  $H_1$ , but when a signature for m such that h(m) = y is requested, reply  $\perp$ .
- $H_3$ : As  $H_2$ , but return 0 if  $[\mathbf{u}_0 + m_1^* \mathbf{u}_1 + \ldots + m_{\mu}^* \mathbf{u}_{\mu}] = [\mathbf{0}]$ .
- $\mathsf{H}_4$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_3$ , but sample  $\mathbf{u}_i \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$  for  $i \in \{0, \dots, \mu\}$ .
- $\mathsf{H}_5$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_4$ , but sample  $A_{2,i} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1,n_2}$  for  $i \in \{0,\ldots,\mu\}$ .
- $\mathsf{H}_6$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_5$ , but sample  $B \leftarrow ^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1,n_2}$ , set  $^{16} M_0 = P_y \cdot B$ ,  $M_i = P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)} \cdot B$  and  $A_{2,i} \leftarrow \mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - M_i \text{ for all } i \in \{0, \dots, \mu\}.$
- $\mathsf{H}_7$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_6$ , but set  $\mathbf{u}_i \leftarrow \alpha_i \cdot \mathbf{u}$  for uniformly sampled  $\alpha_0, \ldots, \alpha_{\mu} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q$ .
- $H_8$ : As  $H_7$ , but set  $sk = (vk, \mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{a}_2)$  and reply to each call  $\mathcal{O}_{sk,Q}(m)$  with  $S.Sign((vk, \mathbf{a}_1), m).$
- $H_9$ : As  $H_8$ , but reply to each call  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{sk},Q}(m)$  with  $\mathsf{S.Sign2}(\mathsf{sk},B,h,y,m)$ , see Figure 10.
- $\mathsf{H}_{10}$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_{9}$ , but sample  $\mathbf{u}_{i} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_{q}^{n_{1}}$  for  $i \in \{0, \dots, \mu\}$ .
- $\mathsf{H}_{11}$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_{10}$ , but sample  $A_{1,i} \leftarrow \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1,n_1}$  for  $i \in \{0,\ldots,\mu\}$ .
- $\mathsf{H}_{12}$ : As  $\mathsf{H}_{11}$ , but set  $A_{1,i} \leftarrow \mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_1$  for  $i \in \{0, \dots, \mu\}$ .
- $H_{13}$ : As  $H_{12}$ , but set  $\mathbf{u}_i \leftarrow \alpha_i \cdot \mathbf{u}$ .

We will prove any adversary for  $H_0$  wins in  $H_1$  with comparable advantage up to a  $1/4\vartheta$  factor loss,  $H_1$  is not harder than  $H_2$ ,  $H_2$  is no harder than  $H_3$  if the DLP is hard and the remaining hybrids are computationally indistinguishable, and winning in  $H_{13}$  is statistically hard.

<span id="page-54-1"></span>i.e.  $(m^*, \sigma^*)$  is a valid forgery and  $m^*$  was not previously queried we denote  $\mathbf{e}_1, \dots, \mathbf{e}_{\mu}$  the canonical base of  $\mathbb{F}_2^{\mu}$ .

<span id="page-54-2"></span>

{55}------------------------------------------------

```
1: Parse sk = (vk, \mathbf{a}_{1}, \mathbf{a}_{2})

2: Parse vk = (([A_{1,i}], [A_{2,i}], [\mathbf{u}_{i}])_{i=0}^{\mu}, \mathbf{t}, k, \mathbb{G})

3: dp \leftarrow (\mathbb{G}, G, q, k, L, T) // deriving G, q from \mathbb{G}

4: // Evaluate Algebraic Hash

5: [A_{1}^{(m)}] \leftarrow [A_{1,0} + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_{i} A_{2,i}], [\mathbf{u}^{(m)}] \leftarrow [\mathbf{u}_{0} + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_{i} \mathbf{u}_{i}]

6: M_{0} \leftarrow P_{y} \cdot B, M_{i} \leftarrow P_{h(\mathbf{e}_{i})} \cdot B // \in \mathbb{F}_{2}^{n_{1}, n_{2}}

7: M^{(m)} \leftarrow M_{0} + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_{i} M_{i} // \in \mathbb{Z}^{n_{1}, n_{2}}

8: // Corner-case Signatures

9: If [\mathbf{u}^{(m)}] = [\mathbf{0}]: Return \sigma = \mathbf{0}

10: // Preimage-Sampling using \mathbf{a}_{2}

11: \mathbf{x}_{1} \leftarrow \mathbb{F}_{2}^{n_{1}}

12: [\mathbf{v}] \leftarrow [A_{1}^{(m)} \mathbf{x}_{1}] // \in \mathbb{F}_{q}^{n_{1}}

13: (\mathbf{x}_{2}, z) \leftarrow \mathbb{P} PreSamp([\mathbf{v}], [\mathbf{u}^{(m)}], \mathbf{a}_{2}, M^{(m)}, \Omega, \mathbf{t} \oplus \mathbf{x}_{1}, d, dp)

14: Return \sigma = (\mathbf{x}_{1}, \mathbf{x}_{2}, z)
```

Fig. 10: Alternative signature scheme using  $\mathbf{a}_2$  to sign any m such that  $h(m) \neq y$ .

Advantage in  $\mathsf{H}_0, \mathsf{H}_1$ . We show  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{A}}^{\mathsf{H}_0}(\lambda) \leq 4\vartheta \cdot \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{A}}^{\mathsf{H}_1}(\lambda)$ , where the advantage refers to the probability that a given hybrid returns 1 at the end of its execution with  $\mathcal{A}$ . Let  $m_1, \ldots, m_\vartheta, m^*$  be as above,  $Q = \{m_1, \ldots, m_\vartheta\}$  and  $\mathsf{Valid} : \mathsf{S.Vfy}(\mathsf{vk}, m^*, \sigma^*) = 1$ . Then

$$\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{A}}^{\mathsf{H}_1}(\lambda) \ = \ \Pr\left[h(m^*) = y \wedge (\wedge_{i=1}^{\vartheta} h(m_i) \neq y) \wedge m^* \notin Q \wedge \mathsf{Valid}\right]$$
$$\geq \ \frac{1}{4\vartheta} \cdot \Pr\left[m^* \notin Q \wedge \mathsf{Valid}\right] \ = \ \frac{1}{4\vartheta} \cdot \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{A}}^{\mathsf{H}_0}(\lambda)$$

where the inequality is by Lemma 2, which can be applied since  $\ell = \log(2\vartheta)$  and (h, y) are never given to the adversary and are in particular independent from  $m_1, \ldots, m_\vartheta, m^*, \sigma^*$  and vk.

Advantage in  $H_1$ ,  $H_2$ . We describe a reduction  $\mathcal{R}$  of  $H_2$  to  $H_1$ . Given an adversary  $\mathcal{A}^*$  for  $H_1$ , the reduction breaks  $H_2$ . Initially  $\mathcal{R}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{vk})$  runs  $\mathcal{A}^*(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{vk})$ . When  $\mathcal{A}^*$  queries a signature for m,  $\mathcal{R}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{vk})$  forwards the query to get  $\sigma$ . If  $\sigma = \bot$  it aborts, or else it forwards  $\sigma$  to  $\mathcal{A}^*$ . Finally, when  $\mathcal{A}^*$  returns  $(m^*, \sigma^*)$ , so does  $\mathcal{R}$ .

Call r the random coins of  $\mathcal{A}^*$  and  $\rho$  those of  $\mathsf{H}_1$ . Without loss of generality we can assume r to also be the random coins of  $\mathcal{R}$ , i.e.  $\mathcal{R}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{vk}; r)$  runs  $\mathcal{A}^*(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{vk}; r)$ . Similarly we can assume  $\rho$  are also the random coins of  $\mathsf{H}_2$ , and that  $\mathsf{H}_1(1^{\lambda}; \rho)$ ,  $\mathsf{H}_2(1^{\lambda}; \rho)$  produce the same  $\mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{sk}, h, y$  and signatures as long as no message  $m_i$  such that  $h(m_i) = y$  is queried. Then for all fixed random tapes  $r = r_0, \rho = \rho_0$ ,  $\mathcal{R}$  wins in  $\mathsf{H}_2$  if  $\mathcal{A}^*$  would win in  $\mathsf{H}_1$ . Indeed if  $\mathcal{A}^*$  would win in  $\mathsf{H}_1$ , then it never

{56}------------------------------------------------

queries  $m_i$  such that  $h(m_i) = y$ . In particular  $\mathcal{R}$  does not abort and returns the same accepting  $(m^*, \sigma^*)$  as  $\mathcal{A}^*$ . Thus  $\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_2}_{\mathcal{R}}(\lambda) \geq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_1}_{\mathcal{A}^*}(\lambda)$ .

Advantage in  $H_2$ ,  $H_3$ . We show the hardness of  $H_2$  reduces to that of  $H_3$  and that of the discrete logarithm problem. Given  $\mathcal{A}$ , we describe  $\mathcal{R}(\mathbb{G}, H_0, \ldots, H_{\mu})$  finding a non-zero linear relation among these group elements, for randomly sampled  $(H_i)_{i=0}^{\mu}$ . The problem is tightly equivalent to the DLP due to random self-reducibility.

Calling  $H_i = [\alpha_i]$ , initially  $\mathcal{R}$  samples  $\mathbf{u}$ ,  $\mathbf{a}_1$ ,  $\mathbf{a}_2$  as per S.Gen, assigning  $[\mathbf{u}_i] = [\alpha_i \mathbf{u}]$  and  $[A_{j,i}] = [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_j]$ . The public key vk is completed sampling  $\mathbf{t}$ , k as in S.Gen and (h, y) as in  $H_1$ . Signature queries are answered with S.Sign(sk, ·). Finally, when  $\mathcal{A}$  returns  $(m^*, \sigma^*)$ , the reduction returns  $(1, m_1^*, \dots, m_u^*)$ .

Notice that by how we defined  $H_3$ , upon fixing the adversary's and hybrid's random coins,  $\mathcal{A}$  wins in  $H_2$  iff it either wins in  $H_3$  or  $[\mathbf{u}_0 + m_1^* \mathbf{u}_1 + \ldots + m_{\mu}^* \mathbf{u}_{\mu}] = [\mathbf{0}]$ . When  $\mathbf{u} \neq \mathbf{0}$  the latter is equivalent to  $H_0 + m_1^* H_1 + \ldots + m_{\mu}^* H_{\mu} = 0$ . Since  $\mathcal{R}$  never returns a zero-vector (the first entry is always 1), calling Win<sub>3</sub> the event in which  $\mathcal{A}$ 's interaction would be accepting in  $H_3$ , then

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_2}_{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) &= \Pr\left[\left[\mathbf{u}_0 + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i^* \mathbf{u}_i\right] = [\mathbf{0}] \vee \mathsf{Win}_3\right] \\ &\leq \Pr\left[\left[\mathbf{u}_0 + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i^* \mathbf{u}_i\right] = [\mathbf{0}]\right] + \Pr\left[\mathsf{Win}_3\right] \\ &\leq \Pr\left[\left[\mathbf{u}_0 + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i^* \mathbf{u}_i\right] = [\mathbf{0}] \wedge \mathbf{u} \neq \mathbf{0}\right] + \Pr\left[\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0}\right] + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_3}_{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \\ &\leq \Pr\left[H_0 + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i^* H_i = 0\right] + \Pr\left[\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0}\right] + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_3}_{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \\ &= \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}}(\lambda) + q^{-n_1} + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_3}_{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_3}_{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda). \end{aligned}$$

Indistinguishability of  $\mathsf{H}_3$ ,  $\mathsf{H}_4$ . Given a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$ , we describe in Figure 11 a reduction  $\mathcal{R}_1$  to  $(n_1, \mu + 1)$ -MDDH. Initially  $\mathcal{R}_1(\mathbb{G}, [C])$  with  $[C] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1, \mu + 1}$  sets  $[\mathbf{u}_i]$  as the *i*-th column of [C]. Then, it computes  $(\mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{sk})$  along with (h, y) as prescribed, setting  $[A_{j,i}] = [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_j]$ , and replies to signature queries via  $\mathsf{S}.\mathsf{Sign}(\mathsf{sk},\cdot)$  with  $\mathsf{sk} = (\mathsf{vk}, \mathbf{a}_1)$ .

By Lemma 9 and Corollary 2  $\mathcal{R}_1$  has negligible advantage in distinguishing a uniformly sampled  $[C_1]$  from  $[C_0]$  with  $C_0 = \mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{w}$  and  $\mathbf{v}$ ,  $\mathbf{w}$  uniformly distributed respectively over  $\mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$  and  $\mathbb{F}_q^{\mu+1}$ .

When  $C = C_0 = \mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{w}$ , then the *i*-th column is of the form  $v_i \cdot \mathbf{w}$  with  $\mathbf{w}$  and  $v_i$  uniformly sampled over  $\mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$  and  $\mathbb{F}_q$ . Hence  $[\mathbf{u}_0], \ldots, [\mathbf{u}_{\mu}]$  are distributed as in  $\mathsf{H}_3$ . Moreover, all the remaining components are correctly computed as a randomized function of  $[\mathbf{u}_0], \ldots, [\mathbf{u}_{\mu}]$ . Hence  $\mathcal{R}_1$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{H}_3$  to  $\mathcal{D}$ .

Conversely, when  $C = C_1$  uniformly sampled, so is every column of C and in particular  $[\mathbf{u}_0], \ldots, [\mathbf{u}_{\mu}]$  are uniformly sampled in  $(\mathbb{G}^{n_1})^{\mu+1}$ . Hence  $\mathcal{R}_1$  simulates perfectly  $\mathsf{H}_4$  toward  $\mathcal{D}$ . We then conclude that

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}_1}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_3,\mathsf{H}_4}(\lambda).$$

{57}------------------------------------------------

```
1: Derive (\mathbb{G}, G, q) from \mathbb{G}
```

2: Parse 
$$[C]$$
 as  $([\mathbf{c}_0], \dots, [\mathbf{c}_{\mu}]) \in (\mathbb{G}^{n_1})^{\mu+1}$  and set  $[\mathbf{u}_i] \leftarrow [\mathbf{c}_i]$ 

3: Sample 
$$h \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell,\mu}$$
 and  $y \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell}$ 

4: 
$$\mathbf{a}_1 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}, \ \mathbf{a}_2 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_2}, \ \mathbf{t} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}, \ k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda})$$

5: 
$$[A_{j,i}] \leftarrow [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_j]$$
 for  $j \in \{1,2\}$  and  $i \in \{0,\ldots,\mu\}$ 

6: 
$$vk = (([A_{1,i}], [A_{2,i}], [\mathbf{u}_i])_{i=0}^{\mu}, \mathbf{t}, k, \mathbb{G}), \text{ sk} = (vk, \mathbf{a}_1)$$

- 7: Run  $\mathcal{D}(vk)$
- 8: When  $\mathcal{D}$  queries  $m \in \{0,1\}^{\mu}$ : Reply  $\sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S.Sign}(\mathsf{sk},m)$  if  $h(m) \neq y$
- 9: When  $\mathcal{D}$  returns b: Return b

Fig. 11: Reduction of the indistinguishability of  $H_3$ ,  $H_4$  to  $(n_1, \mu + 1)$ -MDDH.

Indistinguishability of  $H_4$ ,  $H_5$ . Given a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$  we define in Figure 12 a reduction  $\mathcal{R}_2$  to  $(n_1(\mu+1), n_2+1)$ -MDDH. Initially, given [C],  $\mathcal{R}_2$  isolates the first column  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1(\mu+1)}$ , from the remaining entries  $[V] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1(\mu+1),n_2}$ . Next it splits both matrices vertically into  $\mu+1$  blocks of height  $n_1$ . Precisely

$$[\mathbf{v}] = \begin{pmatrix} [\mathbf{v}_0] \\ \vdots \\ [\mathbf{v}_{\mu+1}] \end{pmatrix} \in (\mathbb{G}^{n_1})^{\mu+1}, \qquad [V] = \begin{pmatrix} [V_0] \\ \vdots \\ [V_{\mu+1}] \end{pmatrix} \in (\mathbb{G}^{n_1,n_2})^{\mu+1}.$$

It then assigns  $[\mathbf{u}_i] = [\mathbf{v}_i]$  and  $[A_{2,i}] = [V_i]$ . Finally, to complete the public key, it samples  $\mathbf{a}_1 \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$  and sets  $[A_{1,i}]$  as  $[\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_1]$ . Signature queries are answered as prescribed in both games with S.Sign(sk, m) where sk = (vk,  $\mathbf{a}_1$ ).

By Lemma 9 and 10, the advantage of  $\mathcal{R}_2$  of distinguishing  $C_1$  uniformly sampled from  $C_0 = \mathbf{v} \otimes (1, \mathbf{w})$  is negligible. When  $C = C_1$  all sub-matrices of C are uniformly sampled. Thus  $[\mathbf{u}_i]$  and  $[A_{2,i}]$  follow the same distribution as in  $\mathsf{H}_5$ , and so  $\mathcal{R}_2(\mathbb{G}, [C])$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{H}_5$ . Conversely, when  $C = C_0 = \mathbf{v} \otimes (1, \mathbf{w})$  we have that  $\mathbf{u}_i$  are uniformly sampled because  $\mathbf{v}$  is, and  $A_{2,i} = \mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{w}$  for a uniformly sampled  $\mathbf{w}$ . Hence  $\mathcal{R}_2(\mathbb{G}, [C_0])$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{H}_4$ . In conclusion

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}_2}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_4,\mathsf{H}_5}(\lambda).$$

Indistinguishability of  $H_5$ ,  $H_6$ . Given a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$ , we detail in Figure 13 a reduction  $\mathcal{R}_3$  to  $(n_1(\mu+1), n_2+1)$ -MDDH. Given [C] it initially isolates the first columns from the other entries, obtaining  $[\mathbf{v}]$ , [V], and then splits both matrices vertically to obtain  $\mu+1$  blocks  $[\mathbf{v}_i]$ ,  $[V_i]$  of height  $n_1$ . As per  $H_6$ , it proceeds sampling B, h, y and computing the matrices  $M_0, M_1, \ldots, M_{\mu}$  in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}$ . It then assigns  $[\mathbf{u}_i] = [\mathbf{v}_i]$  and  $[A_{2,i}] = [V_i - M_i]$ . The verification key is completed sampling  $\mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{t}, k$  as prescribed and setting  $[A_{1,i}] = [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_1]$ . Signature queries for m are again answered with  $\mathsf{S.Sign}(\mathsf{sk}, m)$  with  $\mathsf{sk} = (\mathsf{vk}, \mathbf{a}_1)$ .

By Lemma 9 and 10 the advantage of  $\mathcal{R}_3$  in distinguishing  $C_1$  uniformly sampled from  $C_0 = \mathbf{v} \otimes (1, \mathbf{w})$  is negligible. Moreover,  $C = C_1$  implies  $\mathbf{v}_i, V_i$ 

{58}------------------------------------------------

```
1: Derive (\mathbb{G}, G, q) from \mathbb{G}
         // Split the challenge matrix C in blocks
 2:
         Parse [C] as ([\mathbf{v}], [V]) \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1(\mu+1)} \times \mathbb{G}^{n_1(\mu+1), n_2}
 3:
         Split [\mathbf{v}] into ([\mathbf{v}_0], \dots, [\mathbf{v}_{\mu}]) with [\mathbf{v}_i] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1}
 4:
         Split vertically [V] into ([V_0], \ldots, [V_{\mu}]) with [V_i] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1, n_2}.
 5:
         // Sample the UHF for partitioning
 6:
         Sample h \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{\ell,\mu} and y \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell}
 7:
        // Compute the verification and secret key
 8:
 9: Set [\mathbf{u}_i] = [\mathbf{v}_i] and [A_{2,i}] = [V_i]
        Sample \mathbf{a}_1 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}, \ \mathbf{t} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}, \ k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda})
10:
        [A_{j,i}] \leftarrow [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_j] \text{ for } j \in \{1,2\} \text{ and } i \in \{0,\ldots,\mu\}
11:
        vk = (([A_{1,i}], [A_{2,i}], [\mathbf{u}_i])_{i=0}^{\mu}, \mathbf{t}, k, \mathbb{G}), \text{ sk} = (vk, \mathbf{a}_1)
12:
         Run \mathcal{D}(vk) // Interact with the distinguisher
13:
         When \mathcal{D} queries m \in \{0,1\}^{\mu}: Reply \sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S.Sign}(\mathsf{sk},m) if h(m) \neq y
14:
          When \mathcal{D} returns b: Return b
15:
```

Fig. 12: Reduction of the ind. of  $H_4$ ,  $H_5$  to  $(n_1(\mu+1), n_2+1)$ -MDDH.

uniformly sampled and in particular so are  $\mathbf{u}_i = \mathbf{v}_i$  and  $A_{2,i} = V_i - M_i$ , since  $M_i$  are independent from C. Thus in this case  $\mathcal{R}_3$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{H}_5$ .

Conversely, for  $C = C_0 = \mathbf{v} \otimes (1, \mathbf{w})$  we have that  $\mathbf{u}_i = \mathbf{v}_i$  and  $A_{2,i} = \mathbf{v}_i \otimes \mathbf{w} - M_i = \mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{w} - M_i$  for a uniformly sampled  $\mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_2}$ . Hence  $\mathcal{R}_3$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{H}_6$ . Therefore

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}_3}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_5,\mathsf{H}_6}(\lambda).$$

Indistinguishability of  $\mathsf{H}_6$ ,  $\mathsf{H}_7$ . Given a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$ , we provide a reduction  $\mathcal{R}_4$  to  $(n_1, \mu+1)$ -MDDH. As done for  $(\mathsf{H}_3, \mathsf{H}_4)$ , given  $C \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1, \mu+1}$ , the adversary parses C into  $\mu+1$  columns of length  $n_1$ , interpret them as  $[\mathbf{u}_0], \ldots, [\mathbf{u}_{\mu}]$ . It then samples  $M_0, \ldots, M_{\mu}$  as specified in  $\mathsf{H}_6$ , along with  $\mathbf{a}_j$  for  $j \in \{1, 2\}$ , from which it computes  $[A_{1,i}] = [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_1]$  and  $[A_{2,i}] = [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - M_i]$ . Finally it completes  $\mathsf{vk}$  with  $\mathbf{t} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$  and  $k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda})$  and replies to signature queries with  $\mathsf{S}.\mathsf{Sign}(\mathsf{sk}, m)$  with  $\mathsf{sk} = (\mathsf{vk}, \mathbf{a}_1)$ .

It readily follows  $\mathcal{R}_4$  simulates  $\mathsf{H}_6$  when C is uniform, while it simulates  $\mathsf{H}_7$  when  $C = \mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{w}$  for freshly sampled  $\mathbf{v}, \mathbf{w}$ . Thus by Lemma 9 and Corollary 2

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}_4}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_6,\mathsf{H}_7}(\lambda).$$

Indistinguishability of  $H_7$ ,  $H_8$ . The two hybrids differ only on the definition of sk. However vk and all signature replies follow the same distribution in the two games, meaning  $H_7$ ,  $H_8$  are perfectly indistinguishable.

{59}------------------------------------------------

```
1: Derive (\mathbb{G}, G, q) from \mathbb{G}
         // Split the matrix C in blocks
  2:
          Parse [C] as ([\mathbf{v}], [V]) \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1(\mu+1)} \times \mathbb{G}^{n_1(\mu+1), n_2}
  3:
          Split [\mathbf{v}] into ([\mathbf{v}_0], \dots, [\mathbf{v}_{\mu}]) with [\mathbf{v}_i] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1}
  4:
          Split vertically [V] into ([V_0], \ldots, [V]_{\mu}) with [V_i] \in \mathbb{G}^{n_1, n_2}.
  5:
          // Sample the UHF and B for the partitioning
  6:
         Sample h \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell,\mu}, \ y \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell}, \ B \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1,n_2}
  7:
          Set M_0 \leftarrow P_y \cdot B and M_i \leftarrow P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)} \cdot B // all in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}
  8:
         // Compute the verification and secret key
  9:
         Set [\mathbf{u}_i] = [\mathbf{v}_i] and [A_{2,i}] = [V_i - M_i]
10:
11: Sample \mathbf{a}_1 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}, \ \mathbf{t} \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}, \ k \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{SUF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda})
        [A_{j,i}] \leftarrow [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_j] \text{ for } j \in \{1,2\} \text{ and } i \in \{0,\ldots,\mu\}
12:
13: vk = (([A_{1,i}], [A_{2,i}], [\mathbf{u}_i])_{i=0}^{\mu}, \mathbf{t}, k, \mathbb{G}), \ \mathsf{sk} = (\mathsf{vk}, \mathbf{a}_1)
         Run \mathcal{D}(vk) // Interact with the distinguisher
14:
        When \mathcal{D} queries m \in \{0,1\}^{\mu}: Reply \sigma \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{S.Sign}(\mathsf{sk},m) if h(m) \neq y
15:
          When \mathcal{D} returns b: Return b
16:
```

Fig. 13: Reduction of the ind. of  $H_5$ ,  $H_6$  to  $(n_1(\mu+1), n_2+1)$ -MDDH.

Indistinguishability of  $H_8$ ,  $H_9$ . Let  $\mathcal{D}$  be a distinguisher for the two hybrids. Its final output bit in the two executions is a deterministic function of its random tape  $\rho$  and its view, that is

$$\mathsf{H}_{8} : \left(\rho, ([A_{1,i}], [A_{2,i}], [\mathbf{u}_{i}])_{i=0}^{\mu}, \mathbf{t}, k, B, h, y, \mathbb{G}, \sigma_{1}, \dots, \sigma_{\vartheta}\right),$$

$$\mathsf{H}_{9} : \left(\widehat{\rho}, ([\widehat{A}_{1,i}], [\widehat{A}_{2,i}], [\widehat{\mathbf{u}}_{i}])_{i=0}^{\mu}, \widehat{\mathbf{t}}, \widehat{k}, \widehat{B}, \widehat{h}, \widehat{y}, \widehat{\mathbb{G}}, \widehat{\sigma}_{1}, \dots, \widehat{\sigma}_{\vartheta}\right).$$

Hence the two games are indistinguishable if the two vectors have negligible statistical distance. To prove this we consider the following two vectors:

$$\text{view} := (\rho, \mathbb{G}, \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{a}_2, (\alpha_i)_{i=0}^{\mu}, \mathbf{t}, h, y, \sigma_1, \dots, \sigma_{\vartheta}) 
\widehat{\text{view}} := (\widehat{\rho}, \widehat{\mathbb{G}}, \widehat{\mathbf{u}}, \widehat{\mathbf{a}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{a}}_2, (\widehat{\alpha}_i)_{i=0}^{\mu}, \widehat{\mathbf{t}}, \widehat{h}, \widehat{y}, \widehat{\sigma}_1, \dots, \widehat{\sigma}_{\vartheta}).$$

Note the actual views of  $\mathcal{D}$  are a deterministic function of respectively view, view, since  $\mathbf{u}_i = \alpha_i \cdot \mathbf{u}$ ,  $A_{1,i} = \mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_1$  and, calling  $M_0 = P_y \cdot B$ ,  $M_i = P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)} \cdot B$ , then  $A_{2,i} = \mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - M_i$ . It then suffices to show that  $\Delta(\text{view}, \widehat{\text{view}}) \leq \text{negl}(\lambda)$ . We prove so studying their entries one by one.

 $\mathsf{H}_8 \approx_s \mathsf{H}_9$ : Trivial components. In both vectors  $(\rho, \mathbb{G}, \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{a}_2, (\alpha_i)_{i=0}^{\mu}, \mathbf{t}, h, y)$  and the respective variables in view follow by construction the same distribution. Hence, defining as "partial view" the vector  $\mathsf{pv}^* = (\rho, \mathbb{G}, \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{a}_1, \mathbf{a}_2, (\alpha_i)_{i=0}^{\mu}, \mathbf{t}, h, y)$ 

{60}------------------------------------------------

and  $\widehat{\mathsf{pv}}^* = (\widehat{\rho}, \widehat{\mathbb{G}}, \widehat{\mathbf{u}}, \widehat{\mathbf{a}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{a}}_2, (\widehat{\alpha}_i)_{i=0}^{\mu}, \widehat{\mathbf{t}}, \widehat{h}, \widehat{y})$  we proceed (in light of Lemma 4) conditioning on  $\mathsf{pv}^* = \mathsf{pv}_0^* = \widehat{\mathsf{pv}}^*$  with  $u_i \neq u_j \neq 0$  for all i < j. Note that since  $\mathbf{u}$  is uniformly sampled and independent from other variables in  $\mathsf{pv}^*$ 

$$\Pr[\exists i : u_i = 0 \lor \exists i \neq j : u_i = u_j] \le \frac{n_1(n_1 + 1)}{2q}.$$

 $\mathsf{H}_8 \approx_s \mathsf{H}_9$ : SUF key. Again  $k, \widehat{k}$  are respectively independent from  $\widehat{\mathsf{pv}}^*$ ,  $\widehat{\mathsf{pv}}$  and by construction they follow the same distribution. Next, as we already fixed  $\mathbb{G}$ ,  $\mathbf{u}$ , let  $\widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^*$  be the set whose existence is implied by Theorem 6. Similarly, having fixed  $\mathbb{G}$ ,  $\mathbf{u}$ ,  $\mathbf{a}_2$ , let  $\mathcal{K}_2^*$  be the set whose existence is guaranteed by Theorem 7. We then proceed conditioning on  $k = k_0 = \widehat{k}$  with  $k_0 \in \widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^* \cap \mathcal{K}_2^*$ . Note that by Theorem 6 and 7

$$\Pr\left[k \notin \widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^* \cap \mathcal{K}_2^*\right] \leq \Pr\left[k \notin \widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^*\right] + \Pr\left[k \notin \mathcal{K}_2^*\right] \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda) + \varepsilon_2(\lambda).$$

 $\mathsf{H}_8 \approx_s \mathsf{H}_9$ : UHF key. In both distributions,  $B, \widehat{B}$  are independent from  $\mathsf{pv}^*, k$  and  $\widehat{\mathsf{pv}}^*, \widehat{k}$ , so their conditional statistical distance is 0. To proceed, we condition  $B, \widehat{B}$  to lie on the intersection of a family of "good" sets of UHFs. For each  $m \in \mathbb{F}_2^{\mu}$ , below define the domain  $\mathcal{U}_m^*$  (as done in Section 3.3) and introduce these sets.

$$\begin{split} \mathcal{U}_m^* &= \{ (\mathbf{x}, z^*) \, | \, \delta_{\min} \left( [A_2^{(m)} \mathbf{x} + z \mathbf{u}^{(m)}] \right) \geq d \} \\ \mathcal{B}_1 &= \{ B \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2} \, | \, \mathsf{rk}(B) = n_1 \} \\ \mathcal{B}_{2,m} &= \left\{ B \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2} \, \middle| \begin{array}{l} (\mathbf{x}, z) \leftarrow^\$ \mathcal{U}_m^*, \; \mathbf{s} \leftarrow^\$ \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1} \\ \Delta((\alpha^{(m)} \cdot (\mathbf{a}^\top \mathbf{x} + z), B \mathbf{x}), (\alpha^{(m)} \cdot (\mathbf{a}^\top \mathbf{x} + z), \mathbf{s})) \leq 2^{-\lambda} \end{array} \right\} \end{split}$$

where  $\alpha^{(m)} = \alpha_0 + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i \alpha_i$  and  $A_2^{(m)} = A_{2,0} + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i A_{2,i}$ . By Lemma 9, and since  $n_2 - n_1 \geq \lambda$  by construction, we have that  $B \notin \mathcal{B}_1$  with probability less than  $2^{n_1 - n_2} \leq 2^{-\lambda}$ . Regarding  $\mathcal{B}_{2,m}$ , in the appendix, Section D.4 we prove through the LHL the following claim.

<span id="page-60-0"></span>Claim 14. 
$$\forall m \in \mathbb{F}_2^{\mu} : \mathbf{u}^{(m)} \neq \mathbf{0} \ then \Pr [B \notin \mathcal{B}_{2,m} | \mathsf{pv}^* = \mathsf{pv}_0^*, k = k_0] \leq 2^{-(\lambda + \mu)}.$$

We proceed conditioning on  $B = B_0 = \widehat{B}$  with  $B_0 \in \mathcal{B}_1 \cap \bigcap_{m \in \mathbb{F}_2^{\mu}} \mathcal{B}_{2,m}$ . In view of Lemma 4 this introduces a statistical error term smaller than

$$\Pr\left[B \notin \mathcal{B}_{1} \cap \bigcap_{m \in \mathbb{F}_{2}^{\mu}} \mathcal{B}_{2,m} \middle| \mathsf{pv}^{*} = \mathsf{pv}_{0}^{*}, \ k = k_{0}\right]$$

$$\leq \Pr\left[B \notin \mathcal{B}_{1} \middle| \mathsf{pv}^{*} = \mathsf{pv}_{0}^{*}, \ k = k_{0}\right] + \sum_{m \in \mathbb{F}_{2}^{\mu}} \Pr\left[B \notin \mathcal{B}_{2,m} \middle| \mathsf{pv}^{*} = \mathsf{pv}_{0}^{*}, \ k = k_{0}\right]$$

$$\leq 2^{-\lambda} + 2^{\mu} \cdot 2^{-\lambda} \cdot 2^{-\mu} = 2 \cdot 2^{-\lambda}.$$

{61}------------------------------------------------

 $\mathsf{H}_8 \approx_s \mathsf{H}_9$ : Signatures. Let  $\mathsf{pv}_i = (\mathsf{pv}^*, k, B, \sigma_1, \dots, \sigma_{i-1})$  and analogously  $\widehat{\mathsf{pv}}_i = (\widehat{\mathsf{pv}}^*, \widehat{k}, \widehat{B}, \widehat{\sigma}_1, \dots, \widehat{\sigma}_{i-1})$ . In the Appendix, Section D.4, we prove that for any  $\mathsf{pv}_{i,0}$  satisfying the previous constraints on  $\mathbf{u}, k, B$  the following claim holds:

<span id="page-61-0"></span>Claim 15. 
$$\Delta(\sigma_{i|\mathsf{pv}_i=\mathsf{pv}_{i,0}},\widehat{\sigma}_{i|\widehat{\mathsf{pv}}_i=\mathsf{pv}_{i,0}}) \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda) + 2^{-\lambda} + \varepsilon_2(\lambda).$$

By induction and Lemma 4 we get  $\Delta(pv_{\vartheta+1}, \widehat{pv}_{\vartheta+1}) \leq$ 

$$\leq \vartheta(\varepsilon_1(\lambda) + 2^{-\lambda} + \varepsilon_2(\lambda)) + \frac{n_1(n_1 + 1)}{2q} + 2 \cdot 2^{-\lambda} + \varepsilon_1(\lambda) + \varepsilon_2(\lambda)$$

which is negligible. Since  $\mathsf{view} = \mathsf{pv}_{\vartheta+1}$  and  $\widehat{\mathsf{view}} = \widehat{\mathsf{pv}}_{\vartheta+1}$  we conclude that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{H}_8,\mathsf{H}_9}_{\mathcal{D}}(\lambda) \ \leq \ \varDelta(\mathsf{view},\widehat{\mathsf{view}}) \ \leq \ \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Indistinguishability of  $H_9$ ,  $H_{10}$ . Given a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$ , we describe a reduction  $\mathcal{R}_5$  to  $(n_1, \mu + 1)$ -MDDH. The reduction is analogous to that for  $H_3$ ,  $H_4$ : given  $(\mathbb{G}, [C])$  it computes  $\forall \mathbf{k}$  by setting  $[\mathbf{u}_i]$  as the i-th column of [C]. It then computes  $\forall \mathbf{k}$  as in  $H_9$  by sampling  $\mathbf{t}, k, h, y, B$  and setting  $[A_{1,i}] = [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_1]$  and  $[A_{2,i}] = [\mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - M_i]$  where as usual  $M_0 = P_y B$  and  $M_i = P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)} B$  for  $i \geq 1$ .

When  $C = C_0$  uniformly sampled, then  $\mathcal{R}_5$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{H}_{10}$ , since all  $\mathbf{u}_i$  are uniformly distributed in this case. Conversely, when  $C = C_1 = \mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{w}$  for a uniformly sampled  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$  and  $\mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{\mu+1}$ , then  $\mathbf{u}_i = w_i \cdot \mathbf{v}$  and in particular  $\mathcal{R}_5$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{H}_9$ . Since MDDH is assumed to be hard, by Lemma 9 and Corollary 2

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}_5}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_9,\mathsf{H}_{10}}(\lambda).$$

Indistinguishability of  $\mathsf{H}_{10}, \mathsf{H}_{11}$ . Given a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$ , we define a reduction  $\mathcal{R}_6$  to  $((\mu+1)n_1,n_1+1)$ -MDDH. The reduction is analogous to that for  $\mathsf{H}_4, \mathsf{H}_5$ : Given  $(\mathbb{G},[C])$ ,  $\mathcal{R}_6$  parses the matrix as  $[\mathbf{v}],[V]\in\mathbb{G}^{(\mu+1)n_1}\times\mathbb{G}^{(\mu+1)n_1,n_1}$  and then further splits vertical both the matrix and the vector into blocks of height  $n_1$ , i.e.  $[\mathbf{v}_0],\ldots,[\mathbf{v}_{\mu}]$  and  $[V_0],\ldots,[V_{\mu}]$  with  $\mathbf{v}_i\in\mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$  and  $V_i\in\mathbb{F}_q^{n_1,n_1}$ . Next, it computes  $\mathsf{vk}$  sampling  $\mathbf{a}_2,\mathbf{t},k,h,y,B$  and setting  $[\mathbf{u}_i]=[\mathbf{v}_i],[A_{1,i}]=[V_i]$ .

When  $C = C_0$  is uniformly sampled, then so are  $\mathbf{u}_i$  and  $A_{1,i}$  as in  $\mathsf{H}_{10}$ . Conversely when  $C = C_1 = \mathbf{v} \otimes (1, \mathbf{w})$  for uniformly sampled  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{(\mu+1)n_1}$  and  $\mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{F}_q^{n_1}$ , then  $\mathbf{u}_i$  are again uniformly sampled as prescribed and  $A_{1,i} = \mathbf{u}_i \otimes \mathbf{w}$  as in  $\mathsf{H}_{11}$ . Thus, by Lemma 9 and 10

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}_6}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_{10},\mathsf{H}_{11}}(\lambda).$$

Indistinguishability of  $H_{11}$ ,  $H_{12}$ . Given a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$ , we define a reduction  $\mathcal{R}_7$  to  $((\mu+1)n_1, n_1+1)$ -MDDH. The reduction is identical to  $\mathcal{R}_6$  up to the fact that, with the same notation,  $[A_{1,0}] = [V_0 - I]$  and  $[A_{1,i}] = [V_i]$ . Again by Lemma 9 and 10 it follows that

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}_7}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_{11},\mathsf{H}_{12}}(\lambda).$$

{62}------------------------------------------------

Indistinguishability of  $H_{12}$ ,  $H_{13}$ . Given a distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}$ , we define a reduction  $\mathcal{R}_8$  to  $(n_1, \mu + 1)$ -MDDH. The reduction is identical to  $\mathcal{R}_5$  up to setting  $[A_{1,0}] = [\mathbf{u}_0 \otimes \mathbf{a}_1 - I]$ . By Lemma 9 and Corollary 2, we have

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ = \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{R}_8}(\lambda) \ \ge \ \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}}^{\mathsf{H}_{12},\mathsf{H}_{13}}(\lambda).$$

Hardness of  $H_{13}$ . Given  $\mathsf{vk}$ , we define the following set:  $\mathcal{T} = \{\delta_2([\rho \mathbf{u}]) : \rho \in \mathbb{F}_q\}$ . By construction  $\mathcal{T} \subseteq \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$  has size  $|\mathcal{T}| \leq q$ . Since  $\mathbf{t}$  is uniform over  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ , then

$$\Pr\left[\mathbf{t} \in \mathcal{T}\right] \leq \frac{q}{2^{n_1}} = \frac{1}{2^{\lambda}}$$

because  $n_1 = \lambda + \log q$ . Next we show that  $\mathcal{A}$  could win only when  $\mathbf{t} \in \mathcal{T}$ . If  $\mathcal{A}$  returns  $(m, \sigma)$  such that  $\mathsf{H}_{13}$  returns 1 then m is a valid forgery, that is  $\mathsf{S.Vfy}(\mathsf{vk}, m, \sigma) = 1$  and h(m) = y. To fix notation, let  $\sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z), M_0 = P_y B, M_i = P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)} B$  and  $M^{(m)} = M_0 + m_1 M_1 + \ldots + m_{\mu} M_{\mu} \in \mathbb{Z}^{n_1, n_2}$ . We further denote for clarity  $\bar{M}^{(m)} = (M^{(m)} \mod 2) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}$ . Then the signature satisfies:

$$\mathbf{t} = \delta_2 \left( \left[ A_1^{(m)} \mathbf{x}_1 + A_2^{(m)} \mathbf{x}_2 + z \mathbf{u}^{(m)} + \mathbf{x}_1 \right] \right)$$

$$= \delta_2 \left( \left[ (\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \otimes \mathbf{a}_1 - I) \mathbf{x}_1 + (\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - M^{(m)}) \mathbf{x}_2 + z \mathbf{u}^{(m)} + \mathbf{x}_1 \right] \right)$$

$$= \delta_2 \left( \left[ (\mathbf{a}_1^\top \mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{a}_2^\top \mathbf{x}_2 + z) \cdot \mathbf{u}^{(m)} - M^{(m)} \mathbf{x}_2 \right] \right)$$

$$= \delta_2 \left( \left[ (\mathbf{a}_1^\top \mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{a}_2^\top \mathbf{x}_2 + z) \cdot \mathbf{u}^{(m)} \right] \right) \oplus \bar{M}^{(m)} \mathbf{x}_2.$$

The last step follows by Corollary 1, which we can apply since  $||M^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_2||_{\infty} \le (\mu+1)n_2 \le d-1$ , and because  $\sigma$  being valid implies the following bound:

$$\begin{split} &\delta_{\min}\left(\left[A_1^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}^{(m)}\right]\right) \geq d \quad \Rightarrow \\ &\Rightarrow \ \delta_{\min}\left(\left[A_1^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_1 + A_2^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_2 + z\mathbf{u}^{(m)} + \mathbf{x}_1\right]\right) \geq d - 1 \quad \Rightarrow \\ &\Rightarrow \ \delta_{\min}\left(\left[\left(\mathbf{a}_1^{\top}\mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{a}_2^{\top}\mathbf{x}_2 + z\right) \cdot \mathbf{u}^{(m)} - M^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_2\right]\right) \geq d - 1. \end{split}$$

Next, as h(m) = y in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{\ell}$ , we have that  $\bar{M}^{(m)}$  is zero in  $\mathbb{F}_2^m$ , indeed

$$\bar{M}^{(m)} = P_y B \oplus \left(\bigoplus_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)} B\right) = \left(P_y \oplus \bigoplus_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)}\right) B 
= \left(P_{y \oplus m_1 h(\mathbf{e}_1) \oplus \dots \oplus m_{\mu} h(\mathbf{e}_{\mu})}\right) \cdot B = P_{y \oplus h(m)} B = P_{\mathbf{0}} B = \Omega.$$

where we used the fact that  $P_{(\cdot)}: \mathbb{F}_2^{\ell} \to \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1,n_1}$  is  $\mathbb{F}_2$ -linear (Theorem 2), and so is  $P_{h(\cdot)}: \mathbb{F}_2^{\mu} \to \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1,n_1}$ . In particular

$$\mathbf{t} = \delta_2 \left( \left[ (\mathbf{a}_1^\top \mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{a}_2^\top \mathbf{x}_2 + z) \cdot \mathbf{u}^{(m)} \right] \right) = \delta_2 \left( \left[ \alpha^{(m)} \cdot (\mathbf{a}_1^\top \mathbf{x}_1 + \mathbf{a}_2^\top \mathbf{x}_2 + z) \cdot \mathbf{u} \right] \right)$$

which lies in  $\mathcal{T}$ , where  $\alpha^{(m)} = \alpha_0 + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i \alpha_i$ . We thus conclude that

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{H}_{13}^{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \to 1\right] \leq \Pr\left[\mathbf{t} \in \mathcal{T}\right] \leq 2^{-\lambda}.$$

{63}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-63-0"></span>D.4 Adaptively Secure Scheme: Claims

Proof of Claim 14. At a high level, we first prove that sampling  $(\mathbf{x}, z^*) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{U}_m^*$  when  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)}$  is non-zero, implies  $\mathbf{x}$  has high min-entropy. T hen, we derive the claim combining the leftover hash lemma with an averaging argument.

Regarding the first step, let  $(\bar{\mathbf{x}}, \bar{z}^*) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathsf{Samp}([A_2^{(m)}], [\mathbf{u}^{(m)}], d, \mathsf{dp})$ . By construction, this procedure is equivalent to sampling  $(\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1, \bar{z}_1^*), \ldots (\bar{\mathbf{x}}_{\nu}, \bar{z}_{\nu}^*)$  from  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q$  and return  $(\bar{\mathbf{x}}_I, \bar{z}_I^*)$  with  $I \in \{1, \ldots, \nu, \bot\}$  denoting either the index of the first tuple in  $\mathcal{U}_m^*$ , or  $\bot$ .

Calling  $\alpha^{(m)} = \alpha_0 + \sum_{i=0}^{\mu} m_i \alpha_i$  and  $M^{(m)} = M_0 + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i M_i$ , then  $A_2^{(m)} = \mathbf{u}^{(m)} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2 - M^{(m)}$  where  $\|M^{(m)}\|_{\infty} \leq (\mu + 1)n_2 \leq d$  and  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)} = \alpha^{(m)} \cdot \mathbf{u} \neq \mathbf{0}$ . Moreover by construction  $k = k_0 \in \widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^*$ , therefore by Theorem 6 we derive  $\Pr[(\bar{\mathbf{x}}, \bar{z}^*) = \bot] \leq \varepsilon_1$ . Calling bad the event  $(\bar{\mathbf{x}}, \bar{z}^*) = \bot$  then we have that  $(\bar{\mathbf{x}}, \bar{z}^*)_{|\neg \mathsf{bad}}$  is uniform over  $\mathcal{U}_m^*$ . Hence, projecting on the first component, we have that  $\mathbf{x}$  and  $\bar{\mathbf{x}}_{|\neg \mathsf{bad}}$  are identically distributed. By Lemma 8 and Theorem 13

$$\mathbf{H}_{\infty}\left(\mathbf{x} \mid \alpha^{(m)}(\mathbf{a}_{2}^{\top}\mathbf{x} - z)\right) \geq \mathbf{H}_{\infty}(\mathbf{x}) - \log q = \mathbf{H}_{\infty}(\bar{\mathbf{x}}_{\mid \neg \mathsf{bad}}) - \log q \\
\geq n_{2} - \log \nu + \log(\Pr\left[\neg \mathsf{bad}\right]) - \log q \\
\geq n_{2} - \log 2\lambda - 1 - \log q \\
= n_{1} + 4\lambda + 2\mu$$

where the third inequality follows as  $\Pr[\neg \mathsf{bad}] \geq 1 - \varepsilon_1(\lambda) \geq 1/2$ . Next, since B is uniform over  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1,n_2}$  and independent from  $(\mathbf{x},z^*)$ , Theorem 1 implies that, for a uniformly sampled  $\mathbf{s} \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ 

$$2^{-2\lambda-\mu} \geq \Delta((B, \alpha^{(m)}(\mathbf{a}_2^{\top}\mathbf{x} - z^*), B\mathbf{x}), (B, \alpha^{(m)}(\mathbf{a}_2^{\top}\mathbf{x} - z^*), \mathbf{s}))$$
  
$$\geq \mathbb{E}_B \left[ \Delta((\alpha^{(m)}(\mathbf{a}_2^{\top}\mathbf{x} - z^*), B\mathbf{x}), (\alpha^{(m)}(\mathbf{a}_2^{\top}\mathbf{x} - z^*), \mathbf{s})) \right]$$

Markov inequality thus implies that the statistical distance is larger than  $2^{-\lambda}$  only with probability  $2^{-\lambda-\mu}$ , that is,  $\Pr[B \notin \mathcal{B}_{2,m}] \leq 2^{-\lambda-\mu}$ .

Proof of Claim 15. For simplicity let us denote  $m, \widehat{m}$  the *i*-th message  $\mathcal{D}$  sent in the two executions, and  $\sigma, \widehat{\sigma}$  the reply. Note m is a deterministic function of  $\mathsf{pv}_i$  and  $\widehat{m}$  is the same function of  $\widehat{\mathsf{pv}}_i$ . Hence conditioning on  $\mathsf{pv}_i = \mathsf{pv}_{i,0} = \widehat{\mathsf{pv}}_i$  we have  $m = \widehat{m}$ . Furthermore, as all events considered in this claim will be conditioned to  $\mathsf{pv}_i = \mathsf{pv}_{i,0} = \widehat{\mathsf{pv}}_i$  we omit such condition and instead assume all probability hereafter to be taken in the relative measure conditioning to  $\mathsf{pv}_i = \mathsf{pv}_{i,0} = \widehat{\mathsf{pv}}_i$ .

We notice that if  $\operatorname{pv}_{i,0}$  is such that h(m) = y, then in both games the hybrid returns  $\bot$  and in particular  $\Delta(\sigma, \widehat{\sigma}) = 0$ . Similarly, if  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)} = \mathbf{0}$  then in both games the hybrid returns  $\sigma = \mathbf{0}$  by construction. Hence it only remains to consider those  $\operatorname{pv}_{i,0}$  such that  $h(m) \neq y$  and  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \neq \mathbf{0}$ .

Denote  $\sigma = (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z)$  and  $\widehat{\sigma} = (\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z})$ . Setting  $z^* = z + \mathbf{a}_1^{\top} \mathbf{x}_1$  and  $\widehat{z}^* = \widehat{z} + \mathbf{a}_1^{\top} \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1$ , our goal is to upper bound  $\Delta(\sigma, \widehat{\sigma}) = \Delta((\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z^*), (\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*))$ 

{64}------------------------------------------------

via the key-switching lemma (Lemma 1). Consider the following two functions:

$$f_1: \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1} \to \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1} \qquad \qquad f_1(\mathbf{x}_1) = \mathbf{x}_1 \oplus \mathbf{t}$$

$$f_2: \mathcal{U}_m^* \to \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1} \qquad \qquad f_2(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = \delta_2([A_2^{(m)} \mathbf{x}_2 + z^* \mathbf{u}^{(m)}])$$

 $f_2$  is well defined as any  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) \in \mathcal{U}_m^*$  has finite distance by definition of  $\mathcal{U}_m^*$ .

<span id="page-64-0"></span>Claim 16.  $f_1$  and  $f_2$  are respectively 0 (i.e. perfectly) and  $2^{-\lambda}$ -balanced.

Next, we introduce the following distributions, as in Lemma 1.

- $(\mathbf{v}_2, w^*) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{U}_m^*$  and  $\mathbf{v}_1 \leftarrow^{\$} f_1^{-1}(f_2(\mathbf{v}_2, w^*))$
- $\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1 \leftarrow^{\$} \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$  and  $(\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_2, \widehat{z}^*) \leftarrow^{\$} f_2^{-1}(f_1(\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1))$

The following claims are respectively consequences of Theorem 6 and 7.

<span id="page-64-1"></span>Claim 17. 
$$\Delta((\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z^*), (\mathbf{v}_1, \mathbf{v}_2, w^*)) \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda)$$
.

<span id="page-64-2"></span>Claim 18. 
$$\Delta((\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*), (\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{v}}_2, \widehat{w}^*)) \leq \varepsilon_2(\lambda)$$
.

Assuming the claims hold, we then conclude as  $\Delta(\sigma, \widehat{\sigma}) =$ 

$$= \Delta((\mathbf{x}_{1}, \mathbf{x}_{2}, z^{*}), (\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_{1}, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_{2}, \widehat{z}^{*})) \leq \Delta((\mathbf{x}_{1}, \mathbf{x}_{2}, z^{*}), (\mathbf{v}_{1}, \mathbf{v}_{2}, w^{*})) + \Delta((\mathbf{v}_{1}, \mathbf{v}_{2}, w^{*}), (\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_{1}, \widehat{\mathbf{v}}_{2}, \widehat{w}^{*})) + \Delta((\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_{1}, \widehat{\mathbf{v}}_{2}, \widehat{w}^{*}), (\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_{1}, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_{2}, \widehat{z}^{*})) \leq \varepsilon_{1}(\lambda) + 2^{-\lambda} + \varepsilon_{2}(\lambda)$$

where the first inequality is the triangular inequality, while the second one hold applying the claims to the first and third term, and Lemma 1 to the second one, as  $f_1$  is 0-balanced and  $f_2$  is  $2^{-\lambda}$  balanced.

Proof of Claim 16.  $f_1$  is a bijection, because it is an involution, and in particular 0-balanced. Regarding  $f_2$ , let  $(\mathbf{x}, z^*) \leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{U}_m^*$ . We need to show  $f_2(\mathbf{x}, z^*)$  is  $2^{-\lambda}$ -close to the uniform distribution over  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ .

To this aim, let us we fix more notation for the matrices  $M_i$ . We call  $E_0 = P_y$  and  $E_i = P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)}$ , and  $E^{(m)} = E_0 + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i E_i \in \mathbb{Z}^{n_1, n_1}$ . Then it is immediate to see that  $M^{(m)} = E^{(m)} \cdot B$ . Furthermore, calling  $\bar{M}^{(m)} = (M^{(m)} \mod 2)$  and  $\bar{E}^{(m)} = (E^{(m)} \mod 2)$ , then  $\bar{M}^{(m)} = \bar{E}^{(m)} \cdot B$ .

Since  $B \in \mathcal{B}_{2,m}$  and  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \neq \mathbf{0}$  due to our condition (see the proof of Claim 15), we have that for a uniformly sampled  $\mathbf{s}$  in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ 

$$2^{-\lambda} \geq \Delta((\alpha^{(m)} \cdot (\mathbf{a}_{2}^{\top} \mathbf{x} + z^{*}), B\mathbf{x}), (\alpha^{(m)} \cdot (\mathbf{a}_{2}^{\top} \mathbf{x} + z^{*}), \mathbf{s}))$$
  
 
$$\geq \Delta \left(\delta_{2}([(\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \otimes \mathbf{a}_{2})\mathbf{x} + z^{*}\mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) \oplus (\bar{E}^{(m)}B\mathbf{x}),$$
  
$$\delta_{2}([(\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \otimes \mathbf{a}_{2})\mathbf{x} + z^{*}\mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) \oplus (\bar{E}^{(m)}\mathbf{s})\right).$$

where the second inequality follows applying to each distribution the function mapping  $c_1, \mathbf{c}_2 \mapsto \delta_2([c_1 \cdot \mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) \oplus \bar{E}^{(m)}\mathbf{c}_2$ . To conclude we show the first term

{65}------------------------------------------------

to be  $f_2(\mathbf{x}, z^*)$  and the second one to be uniform in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ . Regarding the first distribution:

$$\delta_{2}([(\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \otimes \mathbf{a}_{2})\mathbf{x} + z^{*}\mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) \oplus (\bar{E}^{(m)}B\mathbf{x})$$

$$= \delta_{2}([A_{2}^{(m)}\mathbf{x} + z^{*}\mathbf{u}^{(m)} + M^{(m)}\mathbf{x}]) \oplus (\bar{M}^{(m)}\mathbf{x})$$

$$= \delta_{2}([A_{2}^{(m)}\mathbf{x} + z^{*}\mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) = f_{2}(\mathbf{x}, z^{*}).$$

The first equality follows since  $A_2^{(m)} = (\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \otimes \mathbf{a}_2) - M^{(m)}$ , and  $\bar{M}^{(m)} = \bar{E}^{(m)}B$ . The second follows by Lemma 3 as  $\|M^{(m)}\mathbf{x}\|_{\infty} \leq (\mu+1)n_2 = d-1$  and the distance of  $[A_2^{(m)}\mathbf{x} + z^*\mathbf{u}^{(m)}]$  is at least d because  $(\mathbf{x}, z^*) \in \mathcal{U}_m^*$ .

Regarding the second term, as **s** is independent from  $\mathbf{x}, z^*$ , it suffices to show  $\bar{E}^{(m)}$  is invertible in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1,n_1}$ . Indeed

$$\bar{E}^{(m)} = P_y \oplus \left(\bigoplus_{i=1}^{\mu} m_i \cdot P_{h(\mathbf{e}_i)}\right) = P_{y \oplus h(m)}$$

where we used the fact that  $P_{(\cdot)}$  and  $P_{h(\cdot)}$  are both  $\mathbb{F}_2$ -linear. Since we assumed  $h(m) \neq y$ , we have that  $y \oplus h(m) \neq 0$ . By Theorem 2 then  $\bar{E}^{(m)}$  is invertible.  $\square$ 

Proof of Claim 17. Recall  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*)$  is the output of  $\mathsf{Samp}([A_2^{(m)}], [\mathbf{u}^{(m)}], d, \mathsf{dp})$ . Let bad be the event  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = \bot$ . Then  $k = k_0 \in \widehat{\mathcal{K}}_1^*$  implies by Theorem 6 that  $\Pr[\mathsf{bad}] \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda)$ . Note the theorem can be applied as we assumed  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)} \neq \mathbf{0}$  and the entries of  $\mathbf{u}$  to be all distinct and all non-zero, implying the same holds for the entries of  $\mathbf{u}^{(m)}$ .

Theorem 4 instead implies that  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*)_{|\neg \mathsf{bad}}$  are uniformly distributed in  $\mathcal{U}_m^*$ . Since  $(\mathbf{v}_2, w^*)$  are uniform in  $\mathcal{U}_m^*$ , we conclude that  $\Delta((\mathbf{x}_2, z^*), (\mathbf{v}_2, w^*)) \leq \Pr[\mathsf{bad}] \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda)$ . Finally, conditioning on  $(\mathbf{x}_2, z^*) = (\bar{\mathbf{x}}_2, \bar{z}^*) = (\mathbf{v}_2, w^*)$  with  $(\bar{\mathbf{x}}_2, \bar{z}^*) \in \mathcal{U}_m^*$ , then by construction

$$\mathbf{x}_1 = \delta_2 ([A_2^{(m)} \bar{\mathbf{x}}_2 + \bar{z}^* \mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) \oplus \mathbf{t} = f_1^{-1} (f_2(\bar{\mathbf{x}}_2, \bar{z}^*)) = \mathbf{v}_1.$$

Therefore  $\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{v}_1$  have zero conditional statistical distance. By Lemma 4 we conclude

$$\Delta((\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{x}_2, z^*), (\mathbf{v}_1, \mathbf{v}_2, w^*)) \leq \Delta((\mathbf{x}_2, z^*), (\mathbf{v}_2, w^*)) \leq \varepsilon_1(\lambda).$$

Proof of Claim 18. Recall that by construction  $\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1$  is uniformly sampled in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ , and in particular  $\Delta(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1) = 0$ . Next, conditioning on  $\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 = \overline{\mathbf{x}}_1 = \widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1$  for all  $\overline{\mathbf{x}}_1 \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}$ , we study the distance of  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*)$  and  $(\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_2, \widehat{w}^*)$ . Recall  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z})$  in S.Sign2 is the output of

$$\mathsf{PreSamp}\left(\left[A_1^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_1\right],\left[\mathbf{u}^{(m)}\right],\mathbf{a}_2,M^{(m)},\varOmega,\mathbf{t}\oplus\mathbf{x}_1,d,\mathsf{dp}\right).$$

Call bad the event  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}) = \bot$ . Then by Theorem 7 Pr  $[\mathsf{bad} \, | \, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 = \bar{\mathbf{x}}_1] \le \varepsilon_2(\lambda)$ , which we can apply because:

<span id="page-65-0"></span>Note that if X and Y are two random variables and bad is an event such that  $\Delta(X|_{\neg \mathsf{bad}}, Y|_{\neg \mathsf{bad}}) = 0$ , then  $\Delta(X, Y) \leq \Pr[\mathsf{bad}]$ .

{66}------------------------------------------------

- $k = k_0 \in \mathcal{K}_2^*$ ;
- $A_1^{(m)}\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1 = \alpha^{(m)} \cdot \mathbf{a}_1^{\top}\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1 \cdot \mathbf{u}$  is parallel to  $\mathbf{u}$ ;
- $\mathbf{u}^{(m)} = \alpha^{(m)}\mathbf{u}$  is parallel to  $\mathbf{u}$  and non-zero due to our assumption (see the proof of Claim 15);
- $(M^{(m)} \mod 2) = P_{y \oplus h(m)} B$  is full rank in  $\mathbb{F}_2^{n_1, n_2}$  since  $y \neq h(m)$  implies  $P_{y \oplus h(m)}$  is invertible and  $B \in \mathcal{B}_1$  is full rank (Theorem 2);
- $||M^{(m)}||_{\infty} \le ||M_0||_{\infty} + \sum_{i=1}^{\mu} ||m_i M_i||_{\infty} \le (\mu + 1)n_2 \le d.$

Next, by Theorem 8 we have  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z})_{|\neg \mathsf{bad}}$  is uniform over

$$\left\{ (\mathbf{x}_0, z_0) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q \, \middle| \, \begin{array}{l} \delta_{\mathsf{min}}([A_1^{(m)} \bar{\mathbf{x}}_1 + A_2^{(m)} \mathbf{x}_0 + z_0 \mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) \ge d \\ \delta_2([A_1^{(m)} \bar{\mathbf{x}}_1 + A_2^{(m)} \mathbf{x}_0 + z_0 \mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) = \mathbf{t} \oplus \bar{\mathbf{x}}_1 \end{array} \right\}$$

Hence  $(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*)_{|\neg \mathsf{bad}}$ , where we recall  $\widehat{z}^* = \widehat{z} + \mathbf{a}_1^\top \overline{\mathbf{x}}_1$ , is uniform over

$$\left\{ (\mathbf{x}_0, z_0^*) \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_2} \times \mathbb{F}_q \left| \begin{array}{l} \delta_{\mathsf{min}}([A_2^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_0 + z_0^*\mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) \geq d \\ \delta_2([A_2^{(m)}\mathbf{x}_0 + z_0^*\mathbf{u}^{(m)}]) = \mathbf{t} \oplus \bar{\mathbf{x}}_1 \end{array} \right\}.$$

Note the first condition is equivalent to  $(\mathbf{x}_0, z_0^*) \in \mathcal{U}_m^*$ , while the second is equivalent to  $f_2(\mathbf{x}_0, z_0^*) = f_1(\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1)$ . Hence  $(\hat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \hat{z}^*)_{|\neg \mathsf{bad}}$  conditioned to  $\hat{\mathbf{x}}_1 = \bar{\mathbf{x}}_1$  is uniform over  $f_2^{-1}(f_1(\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1))$ , and in particular

$$\Delta((\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2,\widehat{z}^*)_{|\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1=\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1},\,(\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_2,\widehat{w}^*)_{|\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1=\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1}) \; \leq \; \Pr\left[\mathsf{bad}|\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1=\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1\right] \; \leq \; \varepsilon_2(\lambda).$$

By Lemma 4 we conclude  $\Delta((\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*), (\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{v}}_2, \widehat{w}^*)) \leq$ 

$$\leq \Delta(\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1, \widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1) + \sum_{\bar{\mathbf{x}}_1 \in \mathbb{F}_2^{n_1}} \Pr\left[\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1 = \bar{\mathbf{x}}\right] \cdot \Delta((\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_2, \widehat{z}^*)_{|\widehat{\mathbf{x}}_1 = \bar{\mathbf{x}}_1}, (\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_2, \widehat{w}^*)_{|\widehat{\mathbf{v}}_1 = \bar{\mathbf{x}}_1}) \leq \varepsilon_2(\lambda).$$

which concludes the proof.
